The Frontispeece explained.
BEhold, the Watchman of the black-brow'd Night,
Who over-looks his charge, by candlelight.
The brats of Hell, by turns do take their places,
To justify their Sins, and plead their cases.
One would be chief, another comes to craveit:
But he that is nearest Hell, is like to have it.
This took most pains, that other gave a price;
The Vmpire pleases all with his device.
The Whore has caught a Gull, (her Scores to pay,)
That Huddles on, to sell himself away.
He leaves his Wife, and Children in distress,
Pimp-wiskin jeares 'em in their heaviness.
The Ruffian-Prodigall, that here you see,
Is come to pay a fine for misery.
He Is in the heat of lust: and he must have
Fuel from Hell to feed it, or a Grave.
But shame and want have cooled the lust, and pride
Of The out-worne Varlet on the other side.
His sinful sweet's congealed into a curse.
Look but a little lower, they are worse:
Where from their sins, into their pains they sall,
Suffering the tortures of the Hospital:
One has his Scull took up, to cleanse his brains
The next is lanced in her bloodlesse veins,
And some are boiling, while the rest do fry:
(All have a taste of Hell, before they die.)
The house that entertained the fowler crew,
Falls, peece-meale down, to render them their due,
Here Is one amazed, another has a maine,
There Is one half dead, lies by another slain.
Both houses punish those that scin'd unseene:
In fine, see how the Devil flies between^
A NIGHTS SEARCH: Discovering the Nature, and condition of NIGHT -- WALKERS
with their associats. digested into a Poem by Hum: Mill.

nocte Latent Mendoe

London: Printed by R: Bishop. for L: B: 1640



A NIGHTS SEARCH. Discovering the Nature and Condition of all sorts of
NIGHT-WALKERS; with their Associates. As also, The Life and Death of many of
them. Together with Diverse fearful and strange Accidents, occasioned by such
ill livers.

Digested into a Poem By HUMPHRY MILL.

Nocte latent mendae; sic sic dixere Poetae;
Ast ego (nec mendax) nocte dieque patent.
LONDON, Printed by Richard Bishop for Laurence Blaicklock at the Sugar-loafe
next Temple-Barre, 1640.

To the Right honorable, ROBERT Earl of Effex, Viscount Hereford, and
Bouchier, Lord Ferrers of Chartley, Bouchier, and Lovaine.
RIGHT HONORABLE,
I Could hardly compose my thoughts, touching the presentation of these my
unworthy endevors, to one so worthy, and truly noble as your Lordship, having
no excuse but your goodness, and the encouragement of those who are truly
honorers of your virtues: Among which number I desire to be known. I have
nothing to plead for my Muse, but that the Subject with the Title, do agree:
And that the fruit she bears, is wholesome, though sour. That which falls with
shaking, is not worth taking up. I know I shall undergo the censure of some,
that can Judge of nothing but what they see; having narrow spirits, only room
for sense at the worst. In respect of such, my Muse was safer walking by Night;
than now by Day. But if it shall please your Lordship to support her with your
hand, and look upon that little good which may appear through a multiplying
glass, and extenuate the weakness, Envy her self will keep at a distance;
and she shall pass with freedom, gayning commendations from your Honors
Approbation. She Is so far adorned with modesty amongst her Opposites, that
she will not bespeak a glance from your eye, but when you be retired from more
weighty occasions. If I should write but what high-borne Fame speaks of your
worth, and call your noble Virtues by their names, I should make the entrance
so pleasing-large, that the Reader would there take up his rest. But it shall
be satisfaction to me, that she is graced with your name: which will preserve
her from living graves. And with my prayers to the God of Truth, for your
Lordships eternal happiness,

I shall rest your most engaged and humble servant, HUMPHRY MILL.

To the Reader.
COURTEOUS READER:
(I Name thee as I would find thee) having had thoughts which often pressed me
to the undertaking of this neglected subject, (not as sinful, but neglected in
the discovery) I could not choose but give entertainment (at last) to so
reasonable a motion: yet not intending to make a work of it, but only to give a
few hints for warning, and Characters to know those devouring locusts, who
strive to spoil all the green plants of the land.

But as I pursued some of them in the chase, I started more of the brood; and
the further I went, the more I found, till at last, I saw necessity of adding
resolution and courage to my purpose; and to search them out for punishment,
which some have done for pleasure. I found many fearful exploits amongst them,
which being but abridged, made these papers swell into such a thickness. Those
that are resolved to follow their lusts, regarding nothing, or spoke or writ,
let them not pass the front of this Book, lest they be tormented before their
time: and suck poison till they burst. I know some like snarling Curres will
bite the Chain, though they break their teeth; and baule at what they
understand not; quarrel with their own shadows, or bark at the man in the
Moon. The painted doxies, and the musk-powd'red Curtizans will be as angry with
me, as Pallas was with the Mice, for haunting her lamp, and gnawing her caul:
and others will cast out smoke and lightning, as Saturnus did among the Armies
of the Frogs and Mice: but I would wish them (as a friend) to say little, what
e're they think, lest they bespeake a smarter whip for themselves. Those that
can digest the sour, need not fear sursetting with the sweet. Let every
reader take what belongs to himself, and pass a gentle Censure on the rest.
The sharpness of it is qualified, where advice takes place, and there are none
that read but may gain, or delight, or profit, more or less, if they stand
not in their own light. I have used their own language in some places to set
out their folly. Though every Phrase or Sentence be not composed of real truth,
yet there is a probability or morality of truth in all. I need not show how a
Metaphorical, Analogicall, or an Allegorical speech retains truth; or how
the figures lead to the substance; or of the liberty of speech that Poetry
affords: In this I had rather learn than teach. I have not used knotty words,
nor rocky expressions in the prosecution of it; but in as suitable terms, as my
slender wit, with so little art, can conceive. Perhaps, the best may find more
than they expect; and the worst more than they desire. My Muse has kept her
self from infection, notwithstanding the many temptations, occasions,
provocations that she has met withal in her search. I suppose. Envy it self
will not question the lawfulness of the subject; seeing the Prophets in diverse
places describe Harlots in their kind; and Solomon that wise King, in their
looks, gestures, speeches, fawning, alluring, etc. before he had any
practical experience of them. But if any ask why must I undertake such a
work? I answer; why should not any man use the gifts that he has received to
profit himself or others? especially if delight put him upon it? or if his
occasions impose not a necessiy of other employments upon him? I was never so
bound to any but I could purchase some time to raise my Genius above these
earthly contents below. For the verses, I have kept to one number and measure,
only the Epitaphs are woven stanza's: In a continued subject, it is not proper
to vary the verse.

I have labored to make the lines so smooth as I could, in placing the
breathing accents where they may fall with most advantage, (except those which
run clean without them) nor have I broke the first, or last foot of the verse,
which might make it run harsh, nor straind the coupling accents above what is
required in the harmony: nor fallen too low in the descent, which might give
the verse an unnatural sound, where the concords should chiefly meet.

If you find any verse a syllable too short in the number, then compare the
measure with it; for if there be a monosyllable that sounds long upon the
letter[r] it will answer to a Spondey, or a Trochey, consisting of two
syllables; especially in the breathing or the coupling accents. If any seem too
long, observe but the cadence, which may fall a syllable longer in the
coupling, yet answer with a true sound: or cls it may fall low, that a Dactyle
which consists of three syllables, bears but the sound of two: and many words
consisting of two, three, four, or five syllables, do naturally fall a
syllable shorter in the pronunciation; or being cut short by Apostrophes,
'twill resolve the doubt. I commit my Muse to your care; her colour's black and
white, you may trust her: she has been purged in the Search, and pressed for
revealing secrets; she will both give and take warning by it, and wishes the
Reader to do the like.

Vale.
Imprimatur: SAM: BAKER. 1640.

To his much respected brother, Master HUMPHRY MILI, upon his Poem called A
Nights Search.
WHen I perused thy smooth and lofty strains,
In this thy Search, the invention of thy brains,
Thy curious language with thy pleasing style,
How thou do praise the honest, lash the vile:
And how thy verses meet, how neatly pointed,
How cleanly limbed, and how exactly joynted;
I could not but commend them; all may see
That Nature has been prodigal to thee.
The best of wit is thine, it is still thy bent
To shame the shameless, clear the innocent.
Thou do in all, so lively act thy part;
Mine eyes did brive mine ears to steal my heart.
Thou have not borrowed what was at a stand,
Nor took up fancies at the second hand;
Nor vamp't an old conceit; nor did thou sit
To stuff in empty words, in stead of wit.
Thy wealthy winged raptures breathe delight
To modest minds; thy end is to affright
All from those odious sins: thou have displaid
The various ways how Young lings are betrayed,
Not like to those that sufet with their bays,
Whose names are far more lasting than their praise.
Those that will twist a wreath for thee,
Must crop the branches of the Cypress tree.
The ancient Poets live in thee again,
To add a lasting glory to thy pen.
Grace beautifies thy parts; but why did hide
Thy worth so long? 'cause thou would not be tried,
By this decrepit age? rich is thy rhyme;
Thy wisdom speaks thee old, before thy time.
'Though those despise thee (that exceptions take)
Whose tongues are dipped in the infernal lake:
The best will honor thec; and thou shall find
Their breath perfumed; thou art not turned with wind,
I thought to chide, but this is all I Will say
Thou camst behind, to steal the wit away.
But I forgive thee: for I doc discern,
Conceited Wits may come to thee and learn.
Though of our name time should the ruin be,
'Twill be revived perpetually in thee.
I needs must love thce, Nature counts it fit:
But reason moves me, to admire thy wit.
Keep off, you vassalls that are wed to lust,
Or here is a Mill will grind you all to dust
THO: MILL Mr of Arts. Oxon.

To my adopted son Mr HUMPHRY MILL, touching his Nights Search.
IN all Night Search 'what can you find
But humor, vapor, smoke, and wind?
Of real goodness then the best.
Is for the most part laid to rest.
They only, whom black night applauds,
As strumpets, panders, thieves, and bawds,
Are open-ey'd; and then each meets
To couple paires in moechall sheets:
Who, least their guilt should come to view,
Have Sentinels that lie perdue,
To keep the viperous brood, such hatch
Both from the Constable, and Watch.
Now, least these should too much abuse
Th'uncautelous world; thy pregnant Muse
Has laid them to the light so plain,
That by the raptures of thy brain,
(If thou son Mill, pursue it still,
And dippe in Helicon thy quill;)
Not one of those thou do display,
Shall dare to show their face by day.
So let those Bats, and Owls that night
Love only, and detest the light,
Into Cimerian darkness run:
For sin did never love the Sun.
More shamed by thy discovery: know,
The Lapidary to make show
Of his best and most orient stone,
A dusky foil it sets upon,
To add to the lusire; so thy Muse
To make its splendour show, did choose
This Argument black and obscure,
To make thy fulgence long endure.
THO: HEYWOOD.

To his kind friend, Master HUMPHRY MILL, upon his special lines of Poetry,
called A Nights Search.
MY kind friend, give me leave to tell you news,
I never in my life was in a Stews:
Nor ever visited a house of sin,
Unless to cure the grinkhams they were in.
I never did by Women set such store,
To leave men's company to see a whore.
Panders and bawds to me have strangers been;
For such procurers, I have never seen
With knowing eyes: till in your book I found them,
Where you have apprehended them and bound them
To answer for their facts: so, truth to tell,
Your booke's no bawdy house, but a Bridewell.
Where they meet punishments upon their merit,
For which your pains do a just praise inherit.
And may all such as lead a life so ill,
Be daily chastised with the laboring Mill.
STEPH: BRADWELL.

To his loving friend, the Author of this Nights Search.
FRiend, how comes this about? what hum'rous fit
Moved thee to make a Constable of thy wit?
Not such as the grave Parish yearly choose
To lead a drowzie Watch, and take abuse
From every drunken gallant: dare not fight,
But when he is guarded on the election night,
Made valiant by the feast; and pays for's cheer
With the shared profits of the following year.
That only makes discovery in the dark,
TO enrich the Justice, and maintain his olarke.
But thou art one (being busy in thy part)
Who ord'rest all with Judgment and with Art.
In one Nights Search thou have discovered more,
Than all the Searchers that have been before.
And which is more, as I believe, do show
Such vice which thy life's practice never did know.
Thy Muse being chaste, and every flowing line
Carr'ing a sense, or moral, or divine,
If understood; though many a clouded mind
Will not the clearness of the intention find.
This subject handled by a Blade oth' times,
That knows to court his less in bawdy rimes,
Would have been renderd odious; he would have shown:
The wicked ills experience made his own.
But thy clean working, on a matter foul,
Has made those ills instruction to the soul.
Examples doe'the wicked chiefly fright,
And nothing's known but by its opposite.
The bad, like Spiders, gather in those fields
Their poison, which to good bees honey yields.
THO: NABBS.

To his kind friend Master HUMPHRY MILL.
UPon thy Search by night, a Search by day
Is made by some; who from thy title say,
What Search should this be? what strange things are done,
That from the glimering candle to the Sun
Are now to be produced? sure, some punke,
Some bawd half-stew'd, some snuffling pander drunk,
Some satin pimp; some plush docoy: me thinks
I see the chain made up of all these links,
With ningle, broker, breaker: and to catch
All these, one man's both Constable and Watch.
And what is all this? must we commend the wit
That spends dear time, and its own strength to sit
Over such a brood as this? the subject's base,
We rather fear, than hope: but pause a space;
And here conceive, the Artists curious hand
May, in'ts impression, at his pleasure stand
In pure, or common matter: yet must shine
In what soever receives it: so thy line
In those course trifles. Briefly, we commend
Not from fair shows, and seeming, but the end
Which wise men still begin withal; begin
With the Authors end, which is the cure of sin,
By ugly sins presentment: and we then
Shall nothing doubt, but you will commend his pen.
Cherish in's Search his laboring Muse, and still
Wish all fair winds breathe o'rethis noble Mill.
THO: BREWER.

Upon the Book, and Author of the Nights Search.
MAny do search and yet for want of light
Are able to discover nothing right:
If thou had wanted light, or sight, even then
Thy Search had been like that of other men.
But being guided by a shining light,
And making use of that thy piercing sight,
Thou have in this one Night, discovered more
Than all thy days, thou ever did before,
Here thou have found out whores, thieves, bawds and panders,
Together with their vassals, and commanders;
And in their lively colors, have discrid,
And painted forth their luxury and pride.
Their cursed traffic for the infernal pit,
And their great labor, more and more to get
Into the mouth of hell; till all at the last
They and their consorts thereinto are cast.
Good ears will listen to thy learned verse
With approbation; and good tongues rehearse
Thy just deserved praise; as for the rest,
That have infected ears, and tongues to jest
At what they understand not; let them still
Be slighted by thee, most Heroic Mill.
THO: GOODEARE Curiae Wardorum.

On the variety of pleasing fancies in the Poem, the Night Search, written by
his friend Mr. HUMPHRY MILL.
YOu that have skill and poetry great store,
That when a line's well drawn how slubberdo're,
Are Patentees of censure, whose high strains,
Do rack the dull perusers lumpish brains
To keep pace with your raptures; deigne awhile,
To listen to the smooth and modest style
Of this industrious Muse, whose merits may
(With your joynt-vote) adorn his brows with bay.
You looser livers who applaud a line
Out-does the ribauld draughts of Aretine;
In whose debauched opinions naught will please
But what is writ in height of wine, and ease,
Whose spleenes are tickeld only at the height,
With some too lust full passage; but will slight
What Is modest and severe; whose searching eye
Graciously smiles on vainer ribauldry:
And by your approbation, praise those lines,
Which both disgrace and eke infect the times.
Draw near and read, perchance you may descry
What may reclaim you from your luxury.
Observe the tricks of the licencious crew,
And with what wiles they plot to ruin you.
Here, see the various cheats at full displaid,
How oft-times by their plots youth is betrayed;
Suppose your selves into like dangers drawn;
Take heed, take heed, your credit lies at pawn.
Read and be wise, for 'twas the Authors care,
That you by these examples should beware.
His Muse meant not to please them, that take pride
To hear a handsome doxy deified;
When all that she is praised for, shall be
The neat contrivance of her letcherie.
How she escapes the watch all hours of night;
The Constable supposing her some sprite,
Dares not affront her; how her chambers be
From the rude violence at Shrove-tide free.
But what the Author touching her shall say,
You Will viewing find it in a differing way.
And if your judgments do conceive him right,
He aims as well to profit as delight.
Peruse him favorably, and you shall see
Much matter handled by his industry;
And when from him you have learned to shun gross crimes,
Be pleased to thank, and praise him for his rimes.
Friend Mill, I could dwell on thy praise; but 'twill
Be a fit subject for some abler quill.
I could commend the smoothness of thy strains,
But it is a work befitteth abler brains
To do it fully: let me therefore be
Only a lover of thy worth and thee.
As it is expected then the learned and wise,
Should this your worth, as I your friendship prize.
Deign your acceptance of these lines, that thence
Your Readers may be taught a lesson hence,
From your example, kindly to accept,
What is devoted to their due respect.
For should you but dislike a line that is here,
It would encourage them to spend a jeere
Upon the lightest criticisme; and take
You for their pattern, who an error make
Of this my forward service; but I fear
These words will prove another trespass here:
And while I labor to abate your ire,
Stirring the embers, I increase the fire.
This, and I Have done; you need not fear its worth,
Only your name will serve to set it forth.
And if the ruder here affrighted gaze
At your unlookt-for raptures, stand amazed:
Let Them awhile but listen, and they will grow
(Being ravished with those numbers from you flow)
In love with wit and fancy: and esteem
You worthy of a laurel Anademe:
Which fame shall crown thy temples with in spite
Of all gaine-sayers of thy Muses right.
Whose worth while other praise, let me admire;
Since, I but add a voice unto their quire.
C. G. ex Oxon.

To the Ingenious Author, Master HUMPHRY MILL, on his book called A Nights
Search.
YOur Mill grindes well; and each pure line
Without a Search may swear it thine.
An obscure subject you display,
In neat expressions clear as day.
Rare Art that can both satisfy
The learned, and the vulgary!
This censure gives a friendly pen,
Thou art the flower of witty men.
Then take this baptism from my quill,
A Mill a Poet, Poet Mill.
Sic approbavit, DAN: FOX. Grayes-Inne.

To his worthy friend the Author of the Nights Search.
IF Decker dockt with discipline and wit,
Gained praises by the Bell-man that he writ;
Or laud on Brath wait waiting did abound;
When a Strappado for the devil he found.
Then may this Mill of Mills, by right of merit,
Equal (if not superior) fame inherit.
Being no Art-invented paper Mill,
That water driving not, lies ever still:
But such a Mill as (in my best account)
Deserves a Mansion on Pernassus mount.
A Mill not agitated (as I find)
Nor set on work by water or by wind.
Nor a poor Edifice by Art compact,
But Nature in this Mill her part did act:
That by self-working of a pan, and pen,
The reputation of luxurious men,
And women, given to soule-killing lust,
Or other vice, grinds as it were to dust;
Making one little world in verse to tell,
What foliyes in this Universe do dwell.
Briefly, this Mill, or Microcosme appears,
In rich endowments old, though young in years:
And though no paper-mill, his worthy name,
This second time pressed paper must proclaim.
His first deserved no carping, nor derision,
For 'twas a useful Melancholy Vision.
And here behold his Nights Search, that may catch
The Constable, perhaps, with all his Watch.
And men in higher office, that are bent
Themselves (and not their Maker) to content;
Whereby the light of reason we may spy,
With Venus, Bacchus, Hermes, many lie.
But were I single (Mill) 'twere best for me,
Next to Divines, still to converse with thee:
In whose vice-hating breast, and active brain,
The Censor Cato seems to live again.
JOAN. PATRIDOPHILUS.

To his judicious friend, Master HUMPHRY MILL, on his Evening Poems.
I Need not win the lookers on to buy
This piece (worth reading over:) every eye
That views thy name to it and has heard thy praise.
Without entreaty will respect the Bays
With favorable perusal: then shall I
Prevent the Readers willing industry;
And as it were, ambitious to be read,
Before thy rare composures, dare to spread
My courser fare at the upper end; but it is
Only to whet their appetites to this:
Which to the second course thou do prepare.
That every palate may commend thy fare.
Taste what you like, and if ought here displease ye,
It Is not because it is bad, but you are queasie.
ROB. NEWTON.

To his friend, HUMPHRY MILL, upon his ensuing Poems.
FRiend, I admire thee, for by heaven I swear,
I have not heard nor seen this many a year,
A subject fitter for my wonder, or
Rather my joy, since flattery I abhor:
For, looking in thy face, I read in thee,
The perfect lines of ingenuitie.
Such is thy work, that thou to fame may wed it,
(By true relation) though I never read it.
Should I conceive thy much aspiring flame
To reach at me? no, I admire the same.
Thy book has more than some will wish indeed,
And that the title does too far exceed.
If any mock the title, say it is flat,
I Will tell him to his teeth, 't's no matter what.
If any say thy lines do cut, or harp
Upon a jest, and say thy wit is sharp;
Thus answer him again, that none but fools
Will be so mad to meddle with edged tools.
To. T. hospitii Lincoln.

To his very good friend, Mr. HUMPHRY MILL, on his Poem called the NIGHTS
SEARCH.
ME thinks, I hear the Punks and Panders say,
There is a Nights Search coming, let us away:
It Is time, I see, there is not a fault has slipped
Away by thee, but has been soundly whipped;
Some burnt in the hand, in the shoulders, elsewhere some;
There Is not a drunkard, filthy queen, or scum,
That Is left unpunisht; nay, it may be said,
Thou have a wit that makes the world afraid.
Men dare not kiss a wench, nor she the men,
For fear of thy two-edged Satyrick pen.
Go on (brave sir) to punish thus the crimes
Of these abusive and adulterous times,
That the age to come may say there was a Mill,
That scowr'd the world from such abortive ill.
Put down your clubs, ye Constables that catch
The leather-winged bats; and you the Watch,
Go stumble home, what needs a rusty bill,
A Welch-hooke, or a halbert? here is a Mill,
That apprehends more Panders, Punks and knaves,
Than all the Beadles with their painted staves:
For this his Nights Search is a piece of work,
That may, for ought we know, convert the Turk:
It Is pity then it should empaled lie,
Within the narrow verge of Britanie:
No, it shall ride in state on Neptunes back,
To the Court of Amurath and Prester Jack.
Who knows what virtue's in it? perhaps his lines
May make them turn away their Concubines.
Grind on brave Mill, thou art for all their frauds:
The great Shrove-tuesdy to the whores and bawds.
ROBERT CHAMBERLAIN.

To the well-deserving, Master HUMPHRY MILL, upon his Poems called A Nights
Search.
Mere flashy Poems best acceptance find,
With men to Novels of the times inclined:
Expressions of a Muse enricht with grace,
(That strikes at sins foul and prodigious race)
Hardly from those deserved praise shall gain,
Who but a thought of good to entertain,
Doloath as death. Be not discouraged then,
If censured by the worthless sort of men.
None that are wise and good, but I dare say,
To read a Poem does so well display
(As yours) the blacknes of these monstrous crimes;
(So freely, boldly practiced in these times:)
And with such art does remedies apply,
To cure the souls each killing malady,
Will much delight and celebrate your praise,
With what is your merits due, the learned Bays.
BAR. PIGOT.

To his friend, Mr. HUMPHRY MILL, upon his rich conceits in his Poems called A
NIGHTS SEARCH.
THe Laws and Statutes which are now in force,
Confirmed by justice, order such a course,
That watchmen guard the night, and while men sleep,
Their goods and persons they in safty keep.
And do (or should) discover noctuall scouts,
As Panders, Pimps, and Bawds, their hellish routs,
And bring them to correction; where the Law
May take effect, and keep them still in awe.
But what is all that to this Nights Search of thine?
Thou have discovered in thy Epick line,
Whole troops of helt-hounds; and thou have displayed
Them to the life; that hell is now afraid
Of thee, thy Genius, and thy searching quill,
Which have revealed another world of ill.
Me thought, I saw thee in thy curious Search,
Much like an Eagle mounted on a perch
To over-looke the world: and having spy'de
A knot of Night-fowles, then thou did divide
Thy nimble feathers; there thou would not stay;
But down thou flew'st, and took'st them for a prey.
Thou did both whip, and teach thy Night-borne prize:
Thou wast too lofty once to stoop for flies.
Yet tenderhearted; thou could not endure
Those that were sore, should go without a cure.
To those that mend not, but will sin again,
The Bridewell lash is easier than thy pen.
Thy book is useful for the Commonwealth,
I love its worth, and thee, and wish thee health.
Mount still with winged raptures; for I know
Thy mind's too spritely (long) to dwell below.
THO: COLLETT.

Upon the deserving Author, Master HUMPHRY MILL, and his Nights Search.
OVt of a drowsy slumber at midnight,
My Muse awakes me, and commands me write
Upon this Nights Search: I was loath to rise;
But rubbed awhile my temples, and mine eyes;
Struck fire, got light, pen, ink, and paper by;
What must I write now? what! yes what? and why?
Must I crowd in among those learned ranks,
That do already overflow the banks
Of this our Mill, with streams from Helicon?
Are there not floods before it many a one,
Of strength enough to drive the wheel about,
But such a weak supply must be drawn out
As I can dribble through a slender quill?
Well, come away; more sacks unto the Mill.
I will make one, but by your leave my Muse,
Stand you aloof: I other aid must use
To praise this work, and workman: to be done
As it should be, by no Muse but his own:
And her I now invoke. Fair modest maid,
Be not in any blush or thought afraid,
That I Will attempt thy Chastity; or wrong
Thee, or thy Poet, though thou' assist my song
In chanting forth his praise; I will not quite
Ravish thee from him: one short hours delight
Intitillations thou can give my brain,
Shall serve my turn; and then fly home again.
Pray be not coy, but, come unto his friend,
Many a man himself so long would lend.
I know he will not be angry: now she comes,
And yields conceit for ten Encomiums,
Were here but space to place them! O she flows,
As if she would turn the whole world out of prose,
Into delightful measures; and the times
Out of rude senses into rationallrimes.
Which of the ancient Poets (with reverence still
Be It spoke) on vices has out-wrote our Mill?
Or of the Modern busy ones who sweeter
Can grind so many mischiefs into meeter?
Write, write apace, all you that boast to be
Traders in Poetry, Prentices, or free,
In praise of this rare Artist; that the earth
May be prepared to welcome this great birth.
This new book of abuses whipped, and stripped,
Which over the witherd old ones head have skipt.
And by its super excellency undone
That which was called the Bel-man too of London.
And here fall down, you Citie-Owles and Bats,
That should watch in the night, (but like dull Cats,
You sleep, or got a birding, while the Rats
Play in the streets before the Counter gates)
Fall down (I say) you Officers of night,
Worship the glory of the Lantern light
That s here held up to you by the hand of Mill,
That shows you the high way to all the ill
He has discoverd; and you should prevent.
Now if you be so ignorantly bent,
Or willfull-blinde, as, we will not see, you say;
You may want eyes to see another day;
Or be so hood-winkt, that you may not spy
When your own wives with other men do lie!
And (under favor) may the City know
What tole or recompense she now does owe
To this industrious Mill, this Watchman? nay
This glory of the night. The City may
Call him her Moonshine; not at least deny
He Is a Night-Dyall to set her Watches by.
Consider now, you that are grave and wise
In City government, and Beadles, Spies,
Informers, and Promooters keep in fee,
The endless unknown worth of Poetry.
Which of enormities discovers more
In one Nights Search than in ten years before;
Had all you Officers compounded for.
And as you love to punish vice, abhor
Ingratitude to virtue; let reward
Engage the Poet then to your regard.
Soshall his Muse your honor sing, and so
Your Mill more merrily the round shall go.
RICHARD BROOM.

To Master MILL, on his NIGHTS SEARCH.
WHere sucb a troop of Worthies do approve,
'Twould seem injustice to conceal the love
I bear thy merits: which should honored be,
By all that do love ingenuitie.
Let me then on these champions of thy fame
Attend; and glory that I have a name
IN the list of thy admirers, and may be
Thought on hereafter for my praising thee.
I am not skilled in complement, nor use
At every trivial cause to rouse my Muse;
Yet truly, I would willingly bestow
That praise upon thee which I justly owe
To thy deserts; but then I must repeat
What others have delivered, and entreat
The loan of some choice fancy, or the wit,
The matter or conceits best suiting it,
From this or that great Artist, lest I run
A new into anobligation.
But I forbear with borrowed coin to pay
The debt I owe, and crave a longer day;
Acknowledging how much our lewder times
Are daily altered by thy well-fill'd rimes.
JO. WILSON, Interioris Templi.

To the Author, M. HUMPHRY MILL, on his worthy work, called A NIGHTS SEARCH.
WHat a good conscience has inspired thy pen,
To throw it is gall upon the faults of men!
And those soule-tempting errors that entice
Loose women to be instruments of vice!
'Twas sure no Constable that is proud to wear
The title of a Parish Officer,
To help his stock, and credit by conniving,
For by severity, there is little thriving.
Nor did a Suburbs Justice teach thee how
To look on sin with a contracted brow;
That brings him profit, and maintains his man,
That writes worse latin than Justinian.
No, no, 'twas zeal in thee, that have refined
Loose Poetry (to lightness more inclined)
And taught it to instruct, not tempt the soul
With wanton raptures, unto acts are foul:
They are by thee so lively made to appear,
Desire dares not attempt them without fear.
What Patron shall reward thee? few there are,
Will to thy full desert a bounty spare.
Those chiefly which delight to spend their means
Upon thy punished bawds and sore-lasht queans.
I wish I could dispose it, thou should be
Master of Bridewell, I so honor thee,
Thou would reform more queans with thy learned quill,
Than beating hemp, or turning of the mill.
THO. N.

Ad amicum fuum candidum, M. HUMPHREDUM MILL, dePoemate faceto, cui Titulus A
NIGHTS SEARCH.
SOme loose-lin'd Rimers by lascivious Lays,
Infect the Air; thou justly bear the Bays,
Thy quill commands a blessed Memory,
Coevall with Long-breathed Eternity:
While thou by Practice and a Poets pen,
Do lash the Brainsick carriages of men;
And so discreetly on a various Matter,
Thy flowing Thoughts most variously do scatter.
Thy Nobler Muse, exites a low-bred strain,
A starved conceit, or fancy from a Swain:
Each verse a rapture is, and every word
A speaking sentence; measures all accord
By due proportion; in this verse of thine
There Is no harsh accent, nor a maimed line.
The sweetened music of thy Newborn lines,
Exceeds old Orpheus pipe, thou charm'st the Times.
While Mirth and Wit, with Modesty make head
To level Vice, and strike profaneness dead,
In this thy Search. That Surg'on wins my heart,
Who if he lance does Anodyze the smart.
What though some Beefe-braines cannot trace thy pen,
But judge thee guilty, as the worst of men!
'Cause their low-fathomes, wedded to their Sense,
Can only judge of things in the Present Tense!
Each Peasant cannot Cube, nor well discry
A Poets Sphere, because his Searching eye
Sublimes it self; we know that spotless name
Is winged abroad to withered Envies shame.
But let it sweli, this truth I Will safely say,
Thy Marshalled Muse has won the field to day;
That when thou pay'st the grave thy debt, To Die,
Will mount thy purchased glory to the sky.
Brave Gallants that swear fealty to sin,
Yield Homage to a lust, or cursed Fien!
This book arrests you; bid your lusts adieu,
Shake hands with Vice, your Mistress, and that Crew;
Or read your Doom with silence, lest you feel
The circling lashes of his Scourge of steel.
Sic approbavit ELIAH PALMER, Londinensis.

To his good friend the Author of the Nights Search.
Feign would I press to drop some lines among
The wits that write thy praise; but being young,
And wanting wit, I fear 'twill be my doom,
My Muse came shuffling in to fill a room.
But my intent is good, that helps the rest;
Thy worth is more than can be here expressed.
Thou have revealed those things, that such as I
May warning take: how do the harlots fry,
And burn alive! thou have displai'da whore
In all her postures; never man did more.
The bawd, the thief, the prodig all, the pimp,
The rascal pander, every hellish Imp
Shall feel thy lash: those that are rotten-ripe,
Drop down before thee; where thou giv'st a stripe,
It Is only 'mendment that can give them ease,
(Join d with thy cures) for their foul disease.
If I should undertake to set thee forth,
I should come short in blazing of thy worth.
But in this work, O! how do thou excel!
I love no flattery, the world can tell.
Thy verse is full of fancy, and thy brain
Drops rich conceits, good language, that thy strain
May please the best of wits; O let it be
Made known to ages what they owe to thee!
PHILIP. CHAMPER NOWNE Medii Templi.

To his respected friend, Master MILL, on his ensuing Poems.
WHen I took pen in hand to write of thee,
An ague took my joints, no part was free:
I would thee and thy Work with praise commend,
But that I knew not when to make an end.
I thought again some palled conceit would fall
Into my quill; then not to write at all
I thought it best; but by and by again,
Me thought I had a fancy in my brain.
With that my barren Muse began to spring,
She somewhat needs would speak, and this's the thing.
How richly is thy Muse adorned, that she
Can sound out things that thou did never see!
She has revealed the plots of hell. By kind
Thou art a Milk indeed, for thoudost grind
Their flesh to naught, and then condemn'st their bones
To be suppressed by hard relentlesse stones.
So full of fancy, decked with rich conceit,
Is all thy Work, by measure, and by weight:
I think that by it daily many a one
Will be reclaimed when thou art dead and gone.
He that does read thy book, will hate the evil,
And speak thy praise, unless it be a devil.
So much is found by thee, that all may say,
Ne re so much known till now (thy night is day.)
Thou do, unfeed, without demurre or pause,
Overthrow the guilty; but thou plead'st the cause
With art and wit, of innocents distressed,
Propounding them away t'attaine to rest;
Preventing all that may be thought or said
Against thee or thy Muse, that hopeful maid,
By all the guard of hell: and thou do bring
Them double shame, in turning back the ^^ing.
How could I stay to tell thy praises over!
My candle's out, now I can write no more.
THO. GITTYNS, Interioris Templi.

A Proeme to the Search, with the occasion of it.
Muse, call thy Genius up, and let thy quill
Prove thee a sharer in the forked hill:
Trace flowery Peneus, taste the silver spring
Of Helicon; scrue up thy treble string:
Begin with co^cords, discords come apace,
And then thou may more freely strike the base.
Let judgment guard thee, reasons counsel ask;
Thou have no common, but a painful task
To undertake; not wanton idle bays:
Love-subjects are too thredbare now adays.
Nor lustful Epigrams, nor jesting rhyme,
Which might corrupt men, rob them of their time:
Nor senseless tales (such trash has venom in it,
No reason; but that sools would be in print)
No, nor the brave exploits of worthy men:
But worthless imps are destined for thy pen.
The bawdy rabble, which have bin protected
By some too often (and punishment neglected)
Must be displaid by thee: their menstr'ous blaince
Launc'd to the quick; their never dying stains
Laid to the view of men; their private ends,
And how they are robbed of reason, wit, wealth, friends;
How vile they make the times, what things did fall
Into the hands of fate, what hellish thrall
Attends upon them; how their shameless breath
At last is sucked into the whirlepoole death.
To those that will return, and will endure
Thy cor'sive, and thy lance, apply thy cure:
Go boldly forth, the Constables will guard thee,
And if thou do good service, they will reward thee.
The watchmen, with the be adles, will attend thee;
Ask light, or weapons of them, they will lend thee;
The Justices will grace thee in thy cause,
And give thee warrants from the strongest laws.
Prepare thy self against tomorrow night,
And be industrious; know, that candlelight
May show thee more than day: for why? the Sun
Does make those night-fowles into corners run.
If any plead for hell, nere favor such;
They have bin favout'd in the world too much.
Search other countries too; for there are weeds
Which harbor serpents, and as hateful deeds
As Britain ever did: lose not thy way,
Thou may be here again by break of day.
Mus. My charge is hard, time short for tedious things!
Thou art a spirit, can not use thy wings?
I never was a subject to this sin;
But now thy search being ready to begin,
I Will help thee in it: and as a stander by
May better judge (casting a serious eye)
Than he that is an actor; so the fame
Of sinful crimes helps me to blaze the shame.
Those that are pincht (perhaps) will fling and kick,
(The gaull'd backed jade will snuff) and seek to pick
A quarrel with thee, and with envy swell;
Though here they are, their venom came from hell:
But answer them at last; none will oppose
Thee in thy Search, unless't be some of those
Which thou have guilty found: and then they will
Show to the world that they are far more ill
Than thou have showed (although they have bin vain)
To peach themselves they will be but fools in grain.
I name'em not; what is at random shown,
In frenzy fits they will make it all their own:
Were they not guilty, they would be possessed
With gentleness, their minds would be at rest.
How ere they rage, yet enter thou their roof,
Though they resist, they are not vengeance proof.
Prevent the bawd, or she will have her desire,
Ere th'watchmen come, she will draw away the fire,
And let her harlots down; such fobs are made
As master-peeces, which do help their trade.
Find out their holes, take but the hearth away,
There shall thou find a hell; the furies play
There in the dark: being apprehended so,
Let no man take a bribe to let them go.
I make no doubt but honest minds will take
The sense at best, and good construction make
Of what is spoke, and meant; and what is weak
They Will silent pass, of what is of worth they will speak.
Hearing the cries of husbands for their wives
Being grown too light (who loved them as their lives.)
Complaints of women, with their grieved moanes,
Their bitter mourning, with their sighs and groans
For their lost husbands; who have bin undone
With following whores: and how poor children run
About for succor, how their brinish tears
Have spoke the parents shame: how free from fears
Vile strumpets walk abroad, how men like flaves
Are chained, and brought untimely to their graves:
How men have lost by trusting such an else,
(I have bin gulled by some of them my self.)
They Will drive men off with words from day to day,
Till they pimp, beg, or die, or run away.
And finding many thus were over-throwne,
I did resolve to make their courses known.
Walking alone over the pleasant fields
Which were enricht with treasure, all which yields
Much matter for delight, 'twas clearly seen
That all the earth was newly clad in green:
It did rejoice that it had cast away.
Its winter suite, which was a suite of gray.
The ayrie Choristers did sweetly sing
Their well-tuned sonnets to the cheerful spring.
Don Phoebus smiled so lovely on the earth,
That it bears twins, or has a second birth.
To make all yet more rich, the gentle showers
With hony-drops resweets the various flowers,
Which now are risen from their frozen beds
To welcome in the Spring: they show their heads
To cheer men's hearts; the milder gales of wind
Alaies the heat, refreshing every mind,
It hardly chides the dust, being calm and fair,
It breathes sweet gusts which do perfume the air.
The beast that was imprisoned and did eat
His bare allowance, now he carves his meat.
The azure-colour heavens joyed to see
Such concord on the earth: the harmony
Possessed me with content; how did this day
Resemble heaven! but, it passed away.
The Charetter of heaven whipped his steeds
That ran too fast before; he never heeds
Our loss, nor care, nor did his fury rest
'Till he had drove them headlong to the west.
The day being slunk away, the season too
Will part ere long (thought I) none can it woo
To tarry here: to qualify our fear,
It promised us a visit every year.
Black Morpheus did begin to chase the light,
I could not call it day, nor was it night:
But being refresht, the notions of my brain
Somewhat revived, homeward I came again.
I heard a cry, and as I passed along
With listening found it was a woman's tongue:
A man replying (made the noise much more)
With railing words, Out of my sight you whore,
I Will travel far enough; What, cannot I
Accompany my friends, but presently
Thou must control me? go thou dirty hag.
I Will bang thy sides, and make thee work, or beg.
The woman's answer modest was, and mild;
Alas, you know that I am big with child;
I Have little ones beside, I am afraid
If you mend not, they will be through want destroid:
You care not for us, nor provide us meat,
We often sit and have no bread to eat:
You spend what ere you get upon a whore,
And now you will be gone to vex me more:
What case am I now in? oh! if you leave me,
I wish the grave more kind, and to receive me.
Then coming to'em, she expressed her grief
To me at large (I gave her some relief)
But he with fury rayl'd, till at the last
A Constable did make him cry as fast;
Who coming, knew the case, where he did dwell;
And to the Justice near to new Bridewell
He had bin brought, but that I made request
For him; he promised to forsake the nest
Of bawdy strumpets, and to end the strife
He did confess his faults, and clear his wife.
Yet afterward this vassal was as bad
As bad might be; which made his wife run mad,
And so she still remains: this gave more strength
Unto my purpose; then my Muse at length
Possessed with fury, pressed with thoughts, grew bold
To bend herself strange stories to unfold.
Though she was angry, she was moved with pity
Toward every one, in Country, Court and City.
It is the sin she strikes at, it is her end
To show examples, others may amend.
These kind of devils (though they range about)
Where once they enter, seldom are cast out.
Her meaning is, in making sharp her pen,
To drive ill spirits out, and save the men.
She fears not envy, Ignoramus least;
One's cursed, but weak, and bayard's but a beast.
If thou do meet her, use her well (she is free)
Or else she may unlookt for meet with thee.
She smiles, yet grieves, to hear how some will roar,
Although they purchase hell to get a whore.
Thy night is come; go, quickly call the watch;
Forth ou have weighty business to dispatch:
Grace honest Poets; as thou walk'st thy round
The wanton Rimer will be quickly found:
Search out the rest, judge as thou find'st their crimes:
Then thou may be a register of times.
A Nights Search.
SECTION 1. The character of a modest, wise Poet, with some touches by the way
at his opposites; his happy end.
THe true borne Poet, that does bend his quill
To scan the world, and finding out the ill,
Provides a cure; and still it is his care
To lance the sore, that others may beware:
He Is temperate, wise, and modest, he will sit
In company to pollish ore the wit.
He Is harmless in his life; no person, place
Are hid from his conceits: he shows that face.
That Is most obscured: his Genius and his pen
May make you think his spirit lives in men.
He Is like a little world; for all things there
Obtain a being in their proper sphere.
All men do meet in him; his searching Art
Sucks in the sweet, and cream of every part,
Gull, knave, or fool, before he will let him pass,
He Will learn the true character of an ass.
He sets out sin (most lively) black as hell,
To fright men from the bait; he can as well
Display't in parts, or gross, or both, or either,
(Though sin and he were never bred together)
As well as any curious painter can
The fashion of a landscape or a man.
The guilty man may read his sin, his shame,
And call it his, although there is not his name:
But virtue in her beauty he has known,
He makes all sure, and takes her for his own:
Then spreads her beauty, that the world may see
She Is lovely in herself; and all may be
Corivals in this match; for she will do
Favors to men, and yet be modest too.
He is a maker, not alone of verse,
But of the matter too; he does rehearse
Much substance in a word: he can compose
His lofty fancies, or in verse, or prose:
But if in verse, how smoothly does it glide
Into the heart? the memory beside
Retains it best: his raptures do translate
The minds of some into a happy state.
His numbers with his measures do agree;
The accents meet with such sweet harmony:
The emphasis is raised with such grace,
That all concurs to keep both time and place.
Good language in his lines he does express,
His couplings join with sense; he is no less
Than heir to Parnassus: he had such a draught
At Helicon, that he is rightly taught
To speak the native tone of all the nine;
But courts Urania, 'cause she is divine.
What ere his measures are, or short, or long,
Lyricks, or Saphicks; if he frames his song
Iambique like, or if Pentameters,
Or double meeters, or Hexameters;
Or if he pitch upon Heroic strains:
'Twill speak his praise, because his seasoned brains
Cast out no dross; he is modest in his line,
What ere his subject be, his worth will shine.
True profit and delight do meet together
In his conceits: although the fool finds neither.
His lines are stored with witty useful pleasure;
Though idiots slight, wise men will prize his treasure.
His company is sweet to those that know
How to make use on it: but he will seldom throw
His breath away upon a scornful ass,
A brute he came, and so he will let him pass.
He takes nor fables, nor conceited dreams,
Nor idle fictions to make up his theames;
Yet he will use them, only to allude
To good, or ill, to shame the multitude.
If melancholy, then he is wise, and grave,
Grief, sorrow, death, are subjects he will have
To work upon; he gives his words by weight;
With vain delights he is quite out of conceit.
If he be pleasant, all his writings tend
To take men with delight: he will commend
A little good, to make'em love the rest:
He Is sad'mongst bad men, merry with the best.
He Will dash an evil out of favor, then
He Will let it blood, but comfort up the men.
He slights the world, nor will he ever be
A favorite to prodigality.
He Is free to all, regarding not his store,
And that is the reason he is often poor.
He hates lascivious rimes, he will not applaud
A fair faced whore, nor yet the common bawd,
But whip'em still; for he will ever pry
In secret places where most dangers lie.
He Is noble-minded (not a sordid else)
He strives to know, and to enjoy himself:
Nor will he flatter great ones for a fee,
Whose worth lies in their wealth, for such as he
Are able to discern: nor will he sawne
Upon his patrons (laying truth to pawn
In every line) unless in him he find
An honest heart graced with a noble mind:
Not like a temporizer, who will hold
Pace with his vices, only for his gold,
Who seribles much, and shameful praise does gain;
T'had better bin undone; for time will stain
His name for ever: most men do detest
All verses for his sake; but yet the rest
Are never the worse; for such this time I borrow:
I have digress'd, I Will speak of him to morrow.
But this ingenious Poet does rehearse
Things as they are, or should be, and his verse
Not stuffed with clouded words, or conjuring strains,
Nor thunderclaps, which might distract the brains
Of honest readers: but in terms most fit
To Express his matter, and to teach them wit.
He does refine conceits, and raise them higher,
His musique's next unto the angels quire.
Nor does he spin it threadbare; he will begin
New fancies as he goes; the spring within
Runs always fresh: he does not trade abroad
With borrowed wit, nor tread the beaten road.
His Genius works when other men do sleep,
His aims are heavenly, and his judgments deep.
He Is humble still, you cannot make him know
His own desert; he is not a man for show.
He does not search for praise, (he loathes all such)
He thinks he is simple, though he knows so much.
But yet to show the vileness of that brood
That do prefer their humors, hate all good,
He Will baffle such men, and he scorns the nest
Of venom-coupledsots: silence is best
To answer such back-biters: he will slight
Detracting vassals that will vomit spite
At what they know not, and will look asquint
On things of worth; what ere has most worth in it
They slubber most with gall; in all that is evil
They Will go as far, and be as like the Devil,
As all their wit can make them: oh! but then
They Will fall with shame before the Poets pen.
Though they like Xerxes whip the sea, and send
A challenge to the hills; yet in the end
The sea's too strong, the mountains are too high
For fools to clamber: so like fools they die.
This honest Poet finds among the wise
His due respect: for they have learned to prize
Persons, and things of worth: and still his bent
Is how to shame the vile, and give content
To all the best. Come, take him as you find him;
He Will think of you, though you do never mind him,
Turn all his verse to prose, it bears the sense
And luster of a poem: and from thence
True worth does spring. The Poets first did teach
Humanity to men, made up the breach,
That rudeness made; all useful Arts were clothed
With Poets wit: why should it then be loathed?
The learned in the languages, rehearse
Much of the sacred text was writ in verse;
As some of Moses law, the Psalms, the Song
Of Solomon, the holy peoples wrong,
Under their foes, by Jeremy related;
The book of Job, and all the songs were stated
In measured Meeters; who would verse disdain,
When Poets have such patterns for their strain!
He that is Dramatick, and does purge the stage
From scurrill dross, and shows this simple age
Their molded trophies; and does always strive
To keep both persons names, and things alive,
His end is good; but idiots learn by this
How to contrive their ways: to do amiss
Some there conclude (of late I heard one say)
I must go meet a whore at such a play.
What pity it is such time, with wit, and cost
Should be bestowed, and prove but labor lost?
This was invented chiefly to be used
By Kings and Nobles, not to be abused
By hackney truls: but now I must return
To lay my honest Poet in his urne:
For having spent his time well, now h'as past
His life to death: the hungry grave at last
Is closed upon him; there he must abide
Until his just and happy cause be tried.
His Epitaph.
YOu sollid stones, incite the gentle dust
To guard this man of worth, that is buried here;
He is a jewel, left unto your trust,
'Till he in glory, gloriously appear.
Though saucie death has laid him in this grave,
His name's alive, and living praise shall have,
SECT. 2. Against lascivious Poets and poetry, and of the ill that is
occasioned by such means.
THou that art skilled in poetry, and wilt
Abuse thy wit, thy parts, to mix the guilt
And filth of lust together, answer here
To what I charge thee with; let shame and fear
Possess thy heart: this first does breed the odds,
Thou striv'st to make the names of heathen gods
Both famous and immortal: always trying
To paint them fresh, and show thy art in lying:
Is It fit a Christian with his muse, his pen,
Should strive to be an ape to heathen men?
Art thou so barren of invention made?
Is wit and fancy low, that thou must trade
Beyond Sea altogether? fictions vain
Must stuff thy verse, and still direct thy strain.
Their feigned gods are guilty (they confess)
Of lust, and rapes, and of all filthiness.
Those Poets with rich nature were endu'd,
Their witty fictions do so much delude
Thee with conceit, that thou have nothing new;
They framed false gods, and worshipped them as true.
Poor men! they roved at random in the dark,
Or natures light, but never had a spark
O'th light above: yet better use they made
Of that, than he who makes his muse turn jade.
I say not but those fictions may be used
To set forth Virtue (not to be abused
To trick up Folly) vices may be shown
In their own colors, and be better kowne,
If well applied: we may leave the dross,
And take the rest, then therein's gain, no loss.
But he that in lascivious strains does glory,
And trims the ruins of a bawdy story,
Does shame his Muse, he strives even to undo her;
She proves a bawd, his wit's a pander to her.
Such poisoned bayts tricked up in gaudy rimes,
Do cheat the simple, and infect the times.
To sinne's too much, more to continue in it,
It Is worse to die and leave his sins in print.
It Is strange! that men who having wit with Art,
Should be so mad to take the devils part!
Does God give men their learning, parts, and wit,
To raise them up, the devils size to fit?
Above the rest, on such he sets a price,
That can with skill paint over his device,
And scribble out a black and hellish roll;
It Is Satan's will: it traps the silly soul.
For any good, he is a stranger to it,
Or plead for truth, he has no heart to do it.
If things of worth do glance but from his pen,
It Is but by chance, he will wish 'em out again.
If he but find one, bred of heavens race,
He strives his cause, or person to disgrace:
And to discourage all that hold with them
Who tread the way toward new Jerusalem.
But if a blade comes, that is to vice inclined,
He Will hugg him, 'cause he only suits his mind,
And he that can to his base humors bend,
(Though nere so bad) he will choose him for his friend
In hindering good, preferring that that is evil,
Is poetry to recompense the Devil?
Will any man esteem that Poet best,
Who in his wanton wit exceeds the rest,
And smooths a path for whores, and does applaud
A shameless villain, and a rotten bawd?
His ulcer'd lines not savouring of the salt,
But raised from burning lust, or wine, or malt,
Do vex the wise; they only cheer all bad men,
Make work for Pimps, and sport for fools and mad men.
A Poets pen should ever strike at vice,
And raise true virtue to a noble price:
And honor truth, dash falsehood out of favor,
Shame foolish Imps, and praise a sweet behavior;
Or else the Devil may a Poet prove,
To honor lust, and give it terms of love.
Of him he borrowes what he does indite,
He would do more, but that he may not write.
It Is like great Pluto has bespoke his quill:
If this be honor, let him have it still.
He does bewitch the youth to bend his mind
To vain delights, (the sting, that comes behind;)
The young man by it is fondly drawn away;
The old man sees vice trimmed, then goes astray:
The modest Maid, whose blushing shows her grace,
She runs away; the whore, whose brazen face
Is varnisht over, she can abide and cry,
Here Is wit at will; it is pity he should die.
The baud she mouths it, sitting on a bench,
A witty Spark, he loves a pretty wench.
Her windows with such pamphlets furnished be,
(The Fiends they dopromote her bawderie)
The VVhiskin laughs until his heart be full;
For with this bait he lately caught a Gull:
Who spent his time to learn this shameless wooing
To sell himself (for fools must needs be doing)
He Is like to thrive, his trade comes on apace,
And all those imps that love the filthy race,
Like Sappho's birds, may chatter out thy praise,
And swear thy scull shall wear the wreath of bays:
The foul that is newly caught will set thee forth,
And sing a catch: oh! here is a man of worth!
Thy Art does teach him to fulfill his lust,
Stirs up the fire, then this vile heap of dust,
Much like an Ape, does on the strumpet grin;
But she so crafty ere he can her win,
Must pay her well: her smiles, and curled locks
Do draw him on; 'till he has got the pox:
Then like a woodcock taken in a snare,
His gold is gone, his empty pare grows bare,
And all his feathers moulted clean away;
And when the Surgeon comes to seek his pay,
This light-brain'd fool, is grown so light in purse,
Except his rot, it is his greatest curse.
He pays no debts, and keeps a hungry table,
He Would run away, but that he is not able.
His sin that promised him such sweet content,
Now stings his soul: the whore when all is spent,
Forsakes him; jearing at his cursed estate;
Her love was false, but perfect is her hate.
Then quickly he begins to stink alive,
While with his gold the queen does seem to thrive:
He Will curse the Poet, rail against his whore;
And being grown so miserably poor,
Disease and want have quenched his lust full flame,
He dies dispairing, leaves a rotten name.
The heavens know if ever he repented;
Amongst men below his death is not lamented;
Excepting those that lived upon his loss,
And's creditors that had so great a cross.
Poor wretched man! that has so short a time!
He Is like a worm that creeps in dust and slime!
Yet spends himself, sin sucking out his marrow;
The world (though wide) does seem for him too narrow;
His brains are nimble to contrive the ill
To please his humors, and his headstrong will.
Those Milldew'd pleasures that delight the sense,
He entertaines; but for his recompense,
Death joins with sin to throw him in his grave,
Dust bars him up, and keeps him like a slave.
His Epitaph.
HEre lics a man bewitched with a whore,
Who spent himself, his time, and his estate:
She slunk away, leaves him to pay the score:
Could any man be more unfortunate?
Now for his debt, Death has attacht his bones,
And keeps them under these bard-hearted stones.
SECT. 3. The wanton Poets Funeral.
THe bawdy Poet's grown as bad as he,
Takes leave of wit, and ingenuitie;
Though he would seem to prove a whore had calling,
She helps him not, although she sees him falling.
Excess of drink, with vileness has overthrown him,
Few friends he has, and some ashamed to own him.
That Morbus which he wished to other men,
Like Eccho's sounding, answers him again.
Great Sol does blush, denies to give him light;
And Luna scorns to come within his sight:
The earth does grumble now to bear him too;
Nor any creature will him service do:
They all disgrace him, he with sin is pressed;
(Shame matched with sorrow, tames both man and beast)
For Celleredge he made his belly large,
And filled it up; but others bore the charge.
And yet (forsooth) he must be counted wise,
Though blasted breath would raise him to the skies,
And his conceit may draw it as his lot,
Yet ere he dies, he turns a very sott:
His land-flood wit that swelled above the brink,
Stole empty air: his soul being like to sink,
His lustful fictions, with the Muses nine,
Affords no help: his sickness is divine.
The inward test, his spirits sad events,
Can find no ease in barren complements.
Like Iordan's streams to Mare Mortuum's lake,
He smoothly glides, a restless rest to take.
Him poverty, and shame does apprehend;
Guilt follows on, and dogs him towered his end;
And having vented all his paultry stuff,
Like Dravtons Moon-calfe, burned to the snuff,
Betwixt extremes, then in his grease he fryes,
'Sparkles a little, and then stinking dies.
From thence his Venus cannot him deliver,
Nor Cupid rescue with his bow, and quiver,
Nor Mars with his valor, no, nor lusty Jove,
(Great Jupiter, will helpless to him prove.)
Swift Mercury and Saturn have agreed,
With all the Gods, to fail him at his need:
Apollo's harp can give him no content;
Parnassus hill, and Helcon have spent
Their cooling drops: bold Bacchus finds no room
To lay a hogshead by him in his tomb.
Ceres can never ease him with the Crop;
The Vote of Chorus brings no healing drop.
But Charon waits to ferry him away,
Where Pluto looks to take him for his prey:
And Cerberus with his winding heads does stretch,
And longs to be the keeper of this wretch.
There Is melody me thinks amongst the throng;
If any mourn, it is 'cause he lived so long.
Sol shows his face with many a pleasant smile,
Since from his sight he is gone that was so vile.
The Moon shines freely, there was none did do her
Such wrong: for he was still an eyesore to her.
The earth is glad she is of her burden eased,
Her furniture is with his absence pleased:
Thus having laid him in the earth so deep,
We'll leave him with the crawling worms to sleep.
His Epitaph.
A Poet lies arrested here by death,
Who honored lust and made it run in rimes;
The world lost nothing but infected breath;
Nor gained he ever by his hateful crimes:
All that he left, or to adorn his hearse,
Or pay his debts, was only bawdy verse.
SECT. 4. The Pimps complaint, the Bawds reply; he changes his course, she
gets another; their life and death.
Pimp. ALas, alas! what mischiefe's here befell?
My friends are dead who loved me very well:
What shall I do! my mind is so at strife!
I Will hang my self, I Am weary of my life.
My place is little worth, my trading's poor,
I spend my vailes, and what I got before
Will soon be gone; this life long cannot hold,
Then poverty will catch me being old.
Provide your self, I Will take some other course:
It Is bad with me, and likely to be worse.

Bawd. Thou silly coxcombe! do thou vex and prate
Hold still thy clack, or else I Will break thy pate.
Have thou forgot when thou wast poor and base,
I took thee in, releiv'd thee in this case?
What wast thou prithee, but a beggars Imp,
When I preferred thee, making thee my Pimp?
Now thou have got good rags upon thy back,
And money in thy purse; thou saucie Jack,
Do dominere? in grumbling in this sort?
Thou slight'st my love, and bringst an ill report
Upon my house: what though those men be dead!
Have I no more that are as bravely bred?
Thou know I have an hundred at the least;
And every one a profitable guest.
All give thee money, feast thee with good cheer;
Thy place is worth fullfifty pounds a year.
But tell me, is it the loss of one or two,
Which makes thee fret, and keep so much ado?
Or else the promise of a larger pay?
Who is it that would inveagle thee away?
Art thou too well? do live too much at ease?
Stay, stay thou fool, and if thou do me please,
When ere I dye, my wealth and household stuff
Shall be thine own; that will be means enough,
If any wench has c^ost thy strong desire,
Let me but know, I Will make her quench thy fire.
But if thou do my favor now refuse,
Go like a sherk, till I shall hear this news
That thou art begging up and down the street;
'T would make me laugh if thee I could but meet.
Thou like a vagrant both to wind and weather,
Shall go as bare as ever thou came hither.
Well, be advised, ere thou do from me part,
I can have choice, but thou will feel the smart.

Pimp. What, are you angry? will you break my head?
I fear you not, I doubt you will creep to bed
Before your time; who then will have the worst?
Of all the world, the bawd is most accursed.
You say you raised me, when you found me poor:
You took me in to man a pockie whore.
To great preferment was I raised then,
A Pimp is still adjudg'd the scum of men.
You call that breeding which does breed men's shame;
And those brave fellows which do use the game
That Is damned to hell; the more they are, the worse;
They snatch the bait but never mind the curse.
Cast up you gains, the profit that comes in,
Hell sets a gloss on it; what is the end of sin?
I thought all well when sin was my delight;
But oh! the wound! how does it now affright
My horrid conscience! now, I hate this thing;
The honey-taste is past, now comes the sting.
It Is not the promise of your wealth that shall
Ensnare my soul in hellish cursed thrall:
Nor yet your threats; I do not fear your charms,
These lofty winds blow me no mortal harms.
Come fair, or foul, there is nothing shall dismay me,
I Will leave my sin, or else it will betray me.
My conscience shall my poverty control,
For th'sweet of sin, I will not sell my soul.
My choice is made, when you your choice do make,
Think on that burning bed, that furious lake
That boiles with brimstone: oh! forsake this way,
Or else the fiends will take you for their prey.
I'had rather beg my bread while here I dwell,
Than beg and howl, and be denied in hell.
Some I have knwne (being taken with a smile)
That served their lusts though time did wink awhile,
It called in death, then thus they make their moan,
The sour is come, but all the sweet is gone!
Seeing such objects bleeding, then thought I
Who would so live, so living would not dye?
As does the Be his progress fly about,
And lights on weeds, and yet sucks honey out:
So have I drawn good from this loath some evil,
And learnd a way how to deceive the devil.
I hate those vermin that do haunt your house,
My vailes, your proffers are not worth a --
I will be freed from't, now I go about it,
Give me free leave, or else I Will go without it.
He being freed from this vile shameful strife,
Does now resolve to lead an honest life.
I wish him happiness; still more to know
What it is to be redeemed from hell below.
Was It ever known that any came from hell?
Or any changed, that once with whores did dwell?
There Is none so bad, that if they can but leave
Their sinful ways, but mercy may receave.
Let none presume, and yet let none despair;
When storms are past, the heavens will be fair.
Muse, stay not here, this convert to applaud
But rurne thy self to shame this shameless bawd.
She melts her grease, as she is running yonder,
With blood-hound sent, to seek a pimp or pander.
She meets a shark, they needs must drink a cup;
He eannot serve: she takes a beggar up;
She changed his rags, and robbed him of his --
And now he is proud, and falls unto his vice
With expedition: having manned a whore,
He gets a fee: now I shall never be poor.
I must be wise (thinks he) though I am bold,
My place is warm, an open barn is cold.
He fits her humor, knowing once her mind,
And she ere long will pay him in his kind.
(They love so strongly, who would have'em parted
Till they'ave been whipped and both together carted?)
He to the bawd spoke thus (and praised his place)

Pimp. Blessed be the time that ere I saw your face,
This is the service I did strive to get,
And by good fortune we together met:
I Am overjoyed that I this place now have;
Who'ld live to beg, and be so poor a slave?
You know my mind, I likewise know your bent,
I make no doubt but I shall give content.
Oh! happy night that brought so sweet a morrow!
Come fill this bowl, I Will drink away all sorrow.

Bawd. If thou prov'st right, thou wilt easily please my mind,
Though I am hasty, thou shall find me kind.
And to my wenches thou must loving be,
And courteous still, that Gentlemen may see
Thou do respect them; they will reward thy pains:
When thou do find thy constant weekly gains,
'Twill cheer thy heart. Look that in any case
Thou art not often absent from thy place.
Never go to Church, for what should thou do there?
'Twill make thee heavy, and bring needless fear.
Nor read, nor study; things being strict and high,
Will break thy brains: why should not thou and I
Live merrily? I have the dainti'st books
Of poetry; if thou but cast thy looks
Upon them once, sadness will steal away;
They make my guests as merry as the day.
And if thou love me, never friendship have
With any that are strict: they will dig a grave
To bury pleasure in; they will prate of death,
Of judgment, hell, and charm thee with their breath.
If any one thou know, that has but coin,
And loves a wench, the gains shall half be thine,
If thou can win him. Do but break a jest
To find his bent; then, never let him rest,
But bring him home; we'll ease him of his cash;
Do It by degrees, thou must not be too rash.
I have two wenches, Kate and little Nell,
They Are pretty rouges, and have their Art so well,
Who ever comes, and does but with them play,
He Is taken: then they make his gold their pray.
These things, and more, hereafter shall thou learn;
Hold up thy head, speak, thou art for my turn.

Pimp. Pray make no question on it; for I Will be true,
And kind to all; but most of all to you.
I have a master that is a joviall blade,
He Is full of gold: I know he loves the trade.
He Will often ask, as I by him do pass,
Where dwells a handsome, young, and trading less?
Do as you promise, let me share a stake,
I Will lay a bait, him we shall quickly take.
When he attends on some fine Curtezan,
And gets a double fee, he is then a man
Much in request: he struts, and cocks his hat,
And has forgot he was a beggars brat.
He Is bold and shameless; that is in him required,
With bawds and whores it is chiefly still desired.
Thus he goes on; the whores do on him smile,
He Is merry-mad: but in a little while
The common Iayles, with Bridewell do consent
To keep this Pimp close to his punishment.
And by degrees, his body with disease
Grows weak; his sin does: fast upon him seize.
His legs do grudge to bear him any longer,
All means are blasted that should make him stronger.
His old familiars he again must have;
Those six-legg'd creepers bring him to his grave;
Whether he died to satisfy the Law,
Or by a hedge, or on a pad of straw;
Or is he tumbled int'a stinking ditch?
It Is one of these, but it is no matter which:
Where e're he lies, there let him still remain,
For if you stir him, he will stink again.
His Epitaph.
HEre lies a beggar that was lately made
A Pimp, to serve a Bawd and man a Whore,
It Is strange a beggar should sorsake his trade!
It made him poorer than he was before.
His bosome-friends returned, to increase his strife,
Conspired with death to take away his life.
And now the bawd is grown so rank in smell,
So stale and hated where she used to dwell;
That she takes leave of joviall merry blithe,
And having quite outlived her hair, her teeth,
(For Partridge, Chickins, Quail, she will often crave it
Worse meat must serve, she will eat when she can have it)
Her custom leaves her now: her household stuff
Must go to pawn for bread: sh'has scarce enough
To feed her craving jaws; her kindred all
Forsake her, loath her; in this shameful thrall
She vomits out her breath. (None ever spied
A gastly'r sight.) And so she sunk and di'd.
Her Epitaph.
'A Woman Bawd who did much mischief hatch,
Did harbor vermin to corrupt the age:
She had a fob to hide them from the watch:
Now she lies plastered in this earthen cage.
Death takes the Drone which used to rob the hive;
She has a grave, who was a grave alive.

SECT. 5. Of two common whores, how they lived and did; and a pimping these,
his life and death.
Muse be not silent, thou have hit the sore,
Refresh thy self, and track the common whore,
Her ways, her wiles, and her declining age,
And her Consorts set with her on the stage.
Their sweet parts acted; cause them to bring in heart,
A just account what profit comes by sin.
This qucane, when pride does once possess her
Or poverty does seem to threaten smart,
Or too much fullness wanton lust does raise,
Or idleness, cause her to take by-ways,
She Is lost for ever. Widow, maid, or wife,
If once she does affect a whorish life.
Then like a Bitch she in her lust will bnrne,
Takes up a rogue, and he must serve the turn.
She walks the fields; more often in the street,
Her bold rude looks, when she a man does meet,
Must make him blush, if he has any grace;
She Is impudent, and will not change her face.
When she is past, observe, and you shall find,
Herbrazen face will cast an eye behind:
She Will fawn, and claim acquaintance with sorne ass,
They must be cozens; then away they pass
Unto some Tavern; with her wiles she will take him,
And ere, they part, a truer Ass she will make him.
He buys his sin, and sels himself away,
And now this whore has made this fool her prey.
When with his wit, his money too is spent,
Then farewell Coz, she leaves him discontent.
She hunts about, and finds some countrey-gull,
Then tries her wits, how she may neatly pull
His feathers off: but when her custom's fading,
She Will take a Shark; for she must needs be trading.
He haunts her still, his sellowes do no less,
She does not own that, that she does possess.
These dunghill flies suck carrion. For their prey
They take by force, or steal her goods away.
(Being desperate villains) if she does but grumble,
They Will beat, and kick her till they make her tumble,
And having had their ends, they leave her bare;
Going to other, there they get a share.
This life they lead, their practice is so ill
They fear the lash, and thus they rob'em still.
Such after-claps, and worse, do chase their pleasure:
(Ill-gotten goods is but deceitful treasure.)
Then ere't be long to Bridewell she is brought,
(She calls it Hell) for there she must be taught
To turn the mill, or beat hemp, choose her which:
If she complains, she is troubled with a stitch,
They Will use a whip to fright this stitch away:
Her gown is blew, and brown bread is her pay.
When she grows old, and time has plowed her brow,
She fills those chinks with painting: think but how
Her painted grease does show; it melts about her:
A shameful object! every boy will flout her.
Then she begins to rattle in the throat,
The harshest discord always is her note;
'Twill rise and fall, like dreamers in their sleep:
And through her nostrils does her language creep.
Now Gallicus is fierce, and takes a skip,
Seizes with fury'pon her upper lip;
Takes part for earnest: you may see her gums,
Her teeth left naked, now she picks up crumbs,
This woman-eater, not content with those,
Does grow so greedy that she eats her nose:
Her eyes are spared, she may reflect, and see
Her monstrous visage, and deformity.
Being noisome; to a cottage she is confined,
Her sap is gone, and nothing left but rind,
That Is shrunk and dry; all hope does quite for sake her:
For better, or for worse, the worms must take her.
Her Epitaph.
HEre lies a strumpet that did use to prey
'Pon any one for gain: at last her sight
Grew odious to the world. She chopped away
Her self: O! Fate, how this might sinners fright!
Old Time grew sick upon it; he loathed her breath,
And he for ease resigned her up to Death.
Another she is grown as bad, or worse:
If her trade fails, she will try to cut a purse,
Or break a house, or cheat; what e're is naught
She Will do: then to the Sessions she is brought:
Arraigned, condemned, she being guilty found:
And ends her da^yes two yards above the ground.
Her Epitaph.
THis shamlesse Imp searched up and down to find
An other world of sin; at last she found
What she would feign have lost; she lest behind
This world: the hangman hid her under ground.
Sh'was overcharged with rope, fast by this place:
Her Motto's this; she died for want of grace.
The villain that did keep her for his whore,
His means is spent; alas! he cannot rore!
He Would borrow cash, but none will take his word,
Then he will swear he will make'em eat his sword.
Now help him at a pinch! he will truly pay
Within a month of the next quarter day:
He Will give his bill, and seal it with his hand,
But he is not guilty of a foot of land.
How happy should he be, had he but coin!
The Broker is resolved to make him fine
Upon receipt of gold: he would in those feathers,
Just like a Turkey, bristle out all weathers.
He Is vexed, but hopes that if the term were come
(With some new trick) he should soon light on some.
If to a place he comes where is but drink,
He Will cock the broom-mans beaver: they must think
He wants not money: by entreaty he
Resolves to grace them with his company.
But if he thinks they'are cowardly and base,
He Will turn his whiskers up, and rub his face,
And seem to take distaste at every word,
He lays his hand, and looks upon his sword;
Stand out on's way; believe me sir he will fight;
(...) blade is old, fit for a man of might.
The Brittons took it when they conquered Wales,
A Captain gin't him coming back from Cales
For his hot service, as a recompense:
It Is hilted since, which cost him thirteen pence.
If one that is valiant does intend to try him,
He Will answer him, (for why should I belly him?)
But he must choose his way: (he'as no desire,
Being burnt before, tolye behind the fire.)
He Is turned to his freedom: first his railing tongue
Must give the onset: that not proving strong,
His heels take place; and he away does run
So soon as ere the combat is begun.
This paultry rascal cheats, and sherks about,
At last some Officer does find him out,
He Is tried; they took his hand against his will,
And made it hiss; me thinks I hear it still.
This graceless wretch will yet no warning take,
But worse, and worse, unlawful shifts he will make;
He breaks a house, and for it he is tried,
Or else for robbing by the highway side.
Then he is condemned to lose his shameless breath:
The time being come, he dies a wooden death.
Before, his whore and he did spit out thunder;
They burned together; but they hang asunder.
His Epitaph.
This sinful clod of earth did still daevise
The pleasant'st way to hell: he had releise
From one who was his whore; but when that prize
Did melt away, and rot; he turned a thief.
His cause was pro and con, and for his life
The halter umpir'd, and did end the strife.
SECT. 6. Course salutations betwixt a pennilesse letcher and his where that
forsook him.
A Man that was addicted to a whore,
His habit showed that he was grown so poor
That he was feign to shark, and shift about;
Amongst his friends he picked a living out.
He did not care so much to be invited,
