And guests are come to accommodate this sinner,
With wanton jests and tales, whilst she is at dinner.
When dinner's done, they will pass the time away
In gaming, or with chat, until the play
Is ready to begin; and there her coat,
And painted face, do make her one of note.
She Will praise a lovesick fancy, but she will vex,
If any word, or act, do touch her sex;
She Is powdered sweetly, which may gain delight;
But coming home, she stinks again at night:
Her face is spoiled: her dressings are laid by;
She Is coffined up again. My Muse and I,
With joint consent, did then her absence crave:
And left her as a restless living grave.
SECT. 22. How an old Bawd lay in for a young Whore: they are found and
punished.
A Private Bawd, that used to entertain
A subtle queen, or two; finding the gain,
She did increase her number: and did learn
How every one might come to take her turn.
She kept a hot-house (which did bring her gold)
Under pretense; but 'twas too hot to hold.
One of her creatures being proved with child,
Having no husband; (sin had so beguiled
This simple slut) and now to hide her shame,
The Bawd must seem with child: and who can blame
Her for it? her husband's very well content
To cover it, all danger to prevent.
Being very big with clouts, she does begin
To fit her self against her lying in:
Her reck'ning's out; her groaning's heard by some,
But ere the Midwife and the neighbors come,
The child is borne; some take distaste and cavil,
And some do wonder at her easy travel!
She seems exceeding weak, and very sick;
The Pander's feign to run and buy a Chick,
To pull't alive, then boy le it to a jelly;
The Nurse approves it good to ease her belly.
Some do mistrust: this the discourse they hold,
Admiring that a woman grown so old
Should have so sweet a babe; but having past
The danger of her month, she is church'd at last.
The whore that bore the child, is free from fear,
(But not from danger, though the coast seems clear)
Unto the Bawd she does her self engage,
And goes by Bridewell boldly; and the cage
Affrights her not; but see, the walls ere long,
(Or something else) begins to find a tongue,
And does bewray the plot: and how they swerved;
And they were punished, as they all deserved.
Was It not enough to sin, thou strumpet vilde,
By prostitut on? and to have a child
Unlawfully be got? but thou must make
Another stranger sin? and to forsake
The infant when 'twas borne? father nor mother
It should have known; but time would never smother
Such monstrous crimes. Poor infant! thou art come
Into the world, to be disgraced by some,
And pitied of the rest! for thou wast brought
Unlooked for hither, by a thing of naught.
Take warning by thy mother; do not sell
Thy soul away, for that which springs from hell.
Thou hateful Pander! how could thou abide
To own this child as thine? how could thou hide
This matchless plot? it is like thy fee was more
For this, than ten times keeping of the door.
Or did they gull thee, making thee believe
It was thine own? my pen should then reprive
Thee from the crime. But thou art weak and cold,
So is thy Bawd, grown impotent and old;
Or did thou do it to keep thy self from trouble?
How could thou think, but this would make it double?
Or did thou take a pride to be a father?
That nere wast one before? but I guess rather
Some gallant got it, and his morgag'd state
Is bound to keep thee at a higher rate.
Come near, thou bawdy hagg; thy wrinkled face
With tawnie surrowes, will thy cause disgrace.
Thou have no wit save only for such tricks,
I think thy age has out-runne sixty six.
Thy hollow eyes have almost lost their sight,
Thou have no color, but thy head is white.
Who gave thy baby suck? once thou had store,
But 'twas at least full forty years before.
Or if some Incubus had lain with thee,
How could thy fruit less than a monster be?
Those that did see thy shape, thy lying in,
Did think that elves and goblins did begin
To nurse their changlings there; when thou did speak
They thought the furies did their gorges break.
Some thought a witch bigg with a little devil
Had come to leave it here, because in evil
It should be more expert: or else in hell
(Being hot and dark) she could not nurse it well.
Some thought that (snake-like) thou would turn young again,
And others thought thou would be a shame to men.
Their fancies differd, as their minds were pressed;
Some feared the worst, and others hoped the best.
Thou desperate wretch! how durst thou go to meet
Him that does know the waverings of thy feet?
Or come within his house? do think thy vow
Is prized of him who knows thou mockst him now?
Do come to give him praise in peoples sight
For safe deliverance? lasting paine's thy right.
Do think he does not know thee? or do think,
Although he knows thee, at this fault he will wink?
He Is never deceived; thou wilt find thy conscience will
Join with the Judge, thy soul with guilt to fill.
You Are ever lost, except thou do repent;
I Will leave thee now to corporal punishment.
SECT. 23. Of a strumpet accusing others, and afterward her self: her
protestations.
WHen wealthy summer did decline in strength,
The golden days diminisht much in length,
The waking time which they from night did take,
Being urged upon it, they restitution make,
To reconcile the ods (but not the crime)
Both day and night must share alike in time:
But light found wary friends to plead for day,
Though she be cast, sh'has a months time to pay.
Men did rejoice because the teeming earth
Was safe delivered of a fruitful birth:
But she looked pale upon it; for all her store
Was strained upon, to pay a twelve months score.
This dealing vexed her, feign she would have chid,
But men had bills, and bands for what they did,
And judgment past upon'em; most unkind!
The birds and beasts gleaned what they left behind.
The watery treasure opened wide its mouth,
And grants a license to the weeping fouth,
For bottling up of tears: the clouds did hold
Their swarthy color, to invite the cold.
The trees grew naked, whistling winds did call
The leaves away. The earth deprived of all
Her newborn treasure, makes a league with men,
For one grain she receives, to pay'em ten
Within the year: she does receive the seed
As earnest ties, to pay as they agreed.
(The fool thought all was buried in a tomb,
Some lived in hope) and then she closed her womb.
But keeping Centry at that time, I spied
A walking clod of earth; and when ^tri'd
My skill, to know what creature this might be,
I found she loved the Art of Bawderie.
She being questioned, did with fury prate,
Till she was locked within the wooden grate.
But being freed again, she did unfold
The names of those, who residence did hold
At such a place; the nature of their meeting,
The occasions on it, their feasting, and their greeting.
What randevous they kept; what domineering,
What time they spent in drinking, whoring, swearing;
How Lady-like one of the queanes lay in;
If any ask, she must not be within;
She Is at her Countrey-house to take the air,
To purge her blood, and make her seem more saire.
Her Serving-man will answer at the door,
(He Is but a dunghill Pimp, to attend this whore)
That he is left to manage her estate,
In black and white; this cancelled Ass will prate.
Another is a Maid, new come to town
To buy a gorger, and a Tabbie gown,
And sell a jewel: she goes all in Plush:
She Is newly entered, and she will quickly blush.
She keeps her chamber, lying backward still;
She now takes physic, and she is very ill.
Another, if you knock, will be afraid,
And she (forsooth) goes for her Chambermaid;
She Will wring her hands, and call the Gods above
To right her cause; she has been crossed in love.
The fourth's a daintiewidow, rich, and young,
Her husband di'd at sea, and she stays long
To gather in her debts: she is wary, staid,
And she is resolved to live without a maid.
A Merchant loves her well, and he does watch
To take her in the mind to strike the match.
All those are common: though they do pretend
This, and the other thing: mark but the end,
You Will find this true, if you have common sense;
Call up the Constable, and fetch'em thence.
The Master's Pander, and the Mistress Bawd;
Take them to Newgate, there they will be awed.
When she had spoke, I followed in the round;
The place was searched, the persons all were found
As she descri'd; the first began to frown
When the Bridewell Matron came to change her gown.
She clad in Plush grew mild, and changed her hue.
She was in black, to morrow she is in blue:
The lovesick maid is from her sweetheart barred,
She Would love the Beadle, but he whips too hard.
The widow learnes good huswifrie, she knocks
The hemp; and yet she is angry with the blocks.
The Merchant leaves her; is it not very ill?
Nor can she stir to prove her husbands will.
The Bawd must ride, the Pander's in disgrace,
Yet he is in hope to get the Hang-mans place.
But afterward this tell-tale whore confessed
Her shameful plots; thus she herself expressed:
It Is true, I am a whore, who knows not that?
I love a private corner, like a batt.
I cut a purse sometimes, and take my share
When others do it; and drink away all care.
But yet I scorn to robb the poor, and when
I do it unknown, I pay'em all again.
Those that can spare it, when they come to me,
(If in our love we can but once agree)
I often pillage them: when they grow bold,
I watch my time to seize upon their gold.
I am no market whore, like those that say
Before they truck, sir, what shall be my pay?
Such mercenary trulls, that will not trade
With any man, until the match be made,
I ever hated: it shall nere be fed
(By any blade) that I am basely bred.
I Will be gentile: with any hide-bound elfe,
I have away how to relieve my self.
Could I wear silk, with gold and silver lace,
A beaver of this price, which gives a grace
To all the rest, a gorget of fine lawne,
Edged round with purle, if I did never pawn
A woodcocks golden feathers? take a prey
When it lies open, ready in my way?
Should I refuse such booties? is it reason?
Farewell, it is late, we will find a fitter season
To drink and laugh: the Constable is true,
The Beadle's right; well, once again, adieu.
See here the vileness of this black-breath'd spirit,
Who thinks her damned sin bespeaks a merit:
It Is true, it merits hell: I fear she will creep
Quite from her grave into the infernal deep.
What Is bad in others, is to her a grace,
It may be she does think to have a place
Of eminence in hell; 'pray has there bin
At any time a nobleness in sin?
She did reveal the meetings of a crew
That leigers were for hell, and lay perdue
To stay the passers by: she thought her trading
Might mend upon it, which by their means was fading,
I hope that sin is of the falling hand,
When hell's divided, long it cannot stand.
Ah! how is this world bewitched! how does it play
With serpents, crocodiles which will destroy
Their own familiars! how does it spend
Its marrow, strength, and time! rush to an end
Which brings in endless woe! oh that my pen
Might strip those beasts! the rest may live like men.
SECT. 24. Of a teeme of hackney Jades, that use to go coupled in the night
along the street.
TO usher in the black and silent night
('It Is often seen) six beasts with collers white,
With measured pace, do walk along the street,
Keeping a distance, mincing with their seet:
Like pamperd mares they prance, spreading their flanks;
Being double-ey'd, they keep both file and ranks.
How stately every one would act her part,
If they might draw together in a cart!
Sometimes demure, and sometimes swell, and foam
Like oxen; but they leave their horns at home,
The two before wear beavers, gowns of silk,
Rich diamond-rings, their hands as white as milk;
Their cheeks new playster'd of a Crimson hue,
The ground well sized, but that their eyes were blew.
No shark durst tempt'em; for it cannot be
That they should yield without a law yers fee.
The second two are full two sizes lower,
They Will take five shillings; if you will give them four,
They Will do for once: the villain will rejoice
That he can have'em both, or take his choice:
Their gowns are Tammy, Grogren, and their rings
Are set with Bristom stones, or some such things.
Their hats are Demicastors, and they'are feign
Touse the courser paint, but's laid in grain.
The last are Wastecoters, they want the trick
To temperdirt, nor can they make it stick;
Or if it do, being greasy, soon 'twill melt,
Their linnen's soul, each of them wears a felt.
They wear Saint Martins stones set well in brass,
They shine i'th'night, and they alike will pass.
They Are eighteen pence a piece; they will abuse
You cheap enough; there is not a -- to choose.
What is their fee, they will not be denied,
You must be sure to pay the shot beside.
If some of them be took, the rest not had,
(The term being done) the night proving but bad,
They know each others haunt, where they will meet
To taste your bounty: (watchmen search the street)
Take on of them, thou wilt take a six fold curse;
Then take thou heed, or they will take thy purse.
This is the way to make a man derided;
By this we know, hell is not yet divided.
SECT. 25. An enquiry, after a young man. that was led away with a whore from
his master.
A Young man lately chanced to be in place,
Where was a Trull, who had a brazen face;
Her words she spoke by gross, her tongue was tip't
With shameless boldness: for 'twas newly dip't
In diverse liquors; but she did protest
The Wine she drank was raught; the Ale was best.
Her carriage with her words did so agree
You Would think that hell had none so bad as she.
This pretty beast found out a silly Ass,
They both agreed to go abroad to grass:
His wit lay in his tongue; all that he had;
But now the Ale had robbed him; drunk, and mad,
He now begins to dally, with this whore,
Through her allurements (so have many more
Been taken by her) and his blood grows warm;
What ere he did, he thought it was no harm.
She having brought him to her cursed bent;
Begins to search what coin he had; what spent:
Judge you his case, for she was much in fear
'Twas low; but then an Angel did appear,
Which cheered her heart: the reckoning being paid;
She draw'd him thence, time must not be delaid,
If place be silent: gone they are together;
(Think what is their end) but who can tell me whither?
Feign would I find them, grant me then your aid,
And get a Warrant; search, for they are strayd
Into some corner: if we find them, so;
But if we cannot, hang 'hem, let them go.
How you may know them, I will now describe,
But if you find them, do not take a bribe,
Nor let them go; but show the Man the Devil;
As for the whore, let Bride well prove this evil.
He Is tallow-fac'd, his eyes are black, and narrow,
His chops are thin, he is mouthed much like a sparrow,
With beetle brows, his brain with humor swims,
Eared like an Ass, his hat has narrow brims,
He Is tall and slender, that me thinks might grace him,
But what is said before does still outface him.
His clothes are very poor, his cloak's the best,
A sad brown color; being once but dressed.
I doubt she will persuade him (else she will rail)
To put it for its faults in Brokers Iyale.
And for the Jade, mark, how you may descry her;
By my description I would not belly her.
Her face is swarty, with a hawks-bill nose,
And goggle-eyes: how such a pair as those
Would fright one in the night! her looks so rude
Would make you wonder, if you are endu'd
With common sense; her mouth of the largest size,
Stands always open, or with lust, or lies.
Her head is almost bald, her teeth are thin:
She, like a changeling, slobbers down her chinne;
Her hands are brawny, and her fingers are
Shrunck up for length, yet swelld with spite, or care:
Her body's gross, her legs do keep asunder,
Her feet are sure, yet being so kept under,
They hew each other; but her legs being big,
Will beare'em out in it, she cares not a fig;
Her knees are reconciled, yet by relation,
They Will part asunder on a light occasion:
Her' parrell is as mean, as mean may be;
Her gown is threadbare, and as light as she.
Her gorgets dirty and I heard some grumbl'd,
She wears no russe, for fear it should be tumbled.
Her Felt is out of fashion; yet 'twill last her
Till trading mend; and then she will have a Caster.
These marks will help you, if you do but mind them;
I Will tell you where too, you are like to find them.
First, search the renters; place, and persons mark,
If there you miss, then go to Mutton park:
For that is a noted place; but yet, I fear
The Bawd being lofty, has no poor puncks there.
Search narrowly; blind Alehouses may be
A cover-slut; let not the horns go free.
Then go to Bloomsbury, and common places
Where varlets dwell, and vermin hide their faces.
From thence to Islington, and seek about
In Alehouses, and Taverns; for a rout
Of vassals there do meet: if that be vain,
And having raised the spirits of the brain,
Come back to Turneboll-street; but have a care,
Be not to bold; for why? such creatures are,
Both tempting, and infectious; I have seen them,
And three in all had but one nose between them.
Then by the way that is about the Playhouse,
Search carefully, for I mistrust the day-house;
If still you misse'em, go to Shorditch then,
For that is a place, where whores have beggerd men:
If there you find them not, I Will say it is strange,
Yet be not out of heart, for Pickt-hatch Grange
Is the most likeliest place: For this I know;
They Are either there, or gone to rotten Row.
Enquiry being made, they are found at last;
The common stock was small; they spent too fast.
The Angel was casheer'd, nor can they rest:
For both their purses, were not penny-blest,
Yet rest they did; but how! their bones were staid;
Ere they are freed, the reckoning must be paid.
Now farewell cloak, it is like to go in trouble;
The sweet is past, the sower will be double!
The whore will car'it; quickly he is drawn
To quit himself, to put his cloak to pawn.
Well, gone it is: he sends it by this whore,
He takes no leave, but never sees it more.
He Is pawned the while, his word they will not take,
Nor can I blame them; now his heart does ache:
Yet not recovered; like a beast he sits:
This whore, and drink, have robbed him of his wits.
This is thy doom, poor fool, thou must abide it!
Thy shame breaks out, thou have no cloak to hide it.
Muse, stay a while, for yonder comes the lade,
And thou shall know, what markets she has made,
She being come, begins to tell her tale;
What pains she took, to put the cloak to sale:
She should have pawned it, for a noble price,
But she has sold it, for a noble thrice.
One part she gave to him; the other two
She kept her self: thus queanes do use to do:
One of the parts, she reckons for her pains;
The other part, she counted for her gains:
This long-lane Broker, well deserves a check,
He judgment gives, and hangs it by the neck:
Its master thought, it should but beare's expenses,
It Is feign to suffer, for his foul offenses!
Wast known by any? has it ever been
That cloaks were punished, for their Masters sin?
This tyrant shows some favor to the Cape,
'Twas proved, that was not guilty of the Rape:
It had revealed them, had it not been blind,
And shamed them too, but that it hung behind.
Then he perceiving how the markets went;
His cloak being gone, and all his money spent,
And he himself is brought to such disgrace,
He Will be but hist at, when he shows his face.
Unto the whore, thus he his mind did utter,
(Shame moved his tongue, he could but only mutter.)
Thou dunghill Jade! thou have undone me quite:
The time's accursed, that I came in thy sight!
Thou tempting witch, thou rotten hag, thou monster!
How vile thou art, the World can never conster;
'Twas thou, 'twas thou, did draw me unto sin,
Thou jear'st me now, what pickle am I in?
Time, where is thy sith? oh! that my glass were run!
I had been blessed, had I not seen the Sun!
With that he stopped, grief had his spirits sunk,
He could not speak; then thus begins the Punk:
Thou prating fool! I took thee for an Ass,
Thou art a Rascal; like a coxcombe pass.
Thou foul-mouthed villain, what, do rail at me?
Go hang thy self, and ease thy misery.
Thou simple drone! doe'st think to lick the honey
In Forma pauperis? have thou spent thy money?
And art thou vexed? who is it can give thee help?
Thou art too young; alas, poor sucking whelp!
Have lost thy cloak? maintained how should I be
Or such as I, but by such fools as thee?
Do cry! hold up thy head: and let me kiss thee,
Kiss somewhere else, if I do chance to miss thee.
Go learn more wit; if thou so can, and then
Get money to it, and come to me again.
Until that time, I will thy absence crave,
This is no jeare; and so adieu poor slave!
He sighs, and weeps, nor can he think of rest:
What dreadful torment does possess his breast:
He Would feign be gone, and yet as feign would stay,
But that his cash is spent, he must away:
But whether knows he not, it is his intent
Home to return; yet fearing to be shent,
His mind does change, he would go then to his kindred,
But he shall shame them; so, his journey's hindered.
If he dos walk by day, his friends will spy him;
Or if by night, the watchmen will desery him:
He giving no account; then they in rage,
Will put him into Bridewell, or the Cage.
His cloak he cannot get, yet goes about it:
Nor has he food, nor can he live without it.
He thus laments his case.
Wretch that I am! was I not worse than mad
To yield to such a whore? I Am worse than bad!
Undone, undone! I have my Fortunes crossed:
My Friends are turned my Foes; my credit's lost.
As I do, who can live: nor can I die:
My death's to live; in what a strait am I!
Me thinks the earth does speak to my disgrace,
The stones, the dust spit venom in my face!
Boreas is angry, growing sharp and thin;
The clowdy Air swells, to revenge my sin!
The Heavens frown, they will not endure my sight:
The days controwler, scorns to give me light!
Could I with these, but once more, have a truce,
And with my Friends, if ever such abuse
I do commit again, then let me be
A model of disgrace; that all by me
May warning take; oh! how do I repent
Of all my baseness! homeward I am bent.
I will submit; what though my master please
For punishment, send me to little ease!
Or else corrects me private? let him do it:
I have deserved it, and I Will yield unto it.
In living thus, it adds unto my sorrow,
Fall back, or edge, home I will go to morrow.
SECT. 26. Of a fight betwixta whore and her Money-lesse guest; and upon what
terms they parted.
ASattin Trull, that had bewitched a man
With her enchantments; (for the Devil can
Fulfill his pimps desires;) (a Conjured spell
Works wonders, strange, being newly brought from hell)
She with her powder, charms, or that black art
Makes him her pray, and seazeth on his heart.
She like a spider, weaves her web so fine,
Faced like an Angel, and her speech divine.
A perfect hypocrite! so full of Sin!
None but the Devil was more full within!
I Will not describe her, with her painted face;
Her Character, is in another place.
This paultry whore lived at so high a rate,
So Lady-like, she must be served in state.
With dainty fare, in plate; she will not fail,
So long as she keeps coying with her tail.
This painted fly spends all that he can get,
For he is took prisoner in the Spiders net:
He thought of nothing, but of sport and play;
At last this fly had worn his wings away.
A captive he remaind, and was content
To undergo't; 'twas sweet imprisonment
In his conceir; but 'twas the Spiders will,
To cast her venom to infect him still.
At last the fly got loose, away went he
Creeping along, because he could not flee.
Often he was caught: because he felt no pain,
He longs to be a prisoner once again;
Ere he had got his strength, or impt his wings,
With golden feathers; diverse other things
Were wanting too: as policy and wit,
Which made this vermin, show a Bedlam fit.
What, come thou here? for what? have brought no money?
Thou simple drone, d^st think to taste the honey?
'Pon quarter ticket? can I sup or dine,
Or pay my rent, except thou bringst me coin?
Words cannot clothe me, they are cheap; but thou
Regardst me not, thou broughtst me nothing now.
Then like a Panther flew she in his face,
Her claws were sharp, her talons did disgrace
His form and visage: he was scratch so sore,
The blood ran out, you would think he would come no more
To visit her; because he felt the smart:
Face, purse, both changed; all will not change his heart;
He begs, entreats, oh! here is a valiant spirit!
She pleads desert, and stands upon her merit.
The co^ne he had, he gave her to be quiet;
But still she swears, five pound's but three days diet.
Must I not feast my friends, to raise my name
From ill report, to credit, love, and fame?
I must be open-handed to the poor,
I must give food and money at my door:
And to my neighbors must not I be free,
To win their love, they may speak well of me?
How should I do it? my love, and not my hands,
Must bring in gains; thou know I have no lands.
Out of my sight! never come to me again,
But bring more gold; I Will trade with other men.
He was perplexed, nor knew he what to say;
But cries for mercy, thinking of a way
To make amends, if she would but forgive him,
He Would send her money; for her words did grieve him.
The under Pimp must go, they did agree,
(Seeing he was moved with her necessity)
That he should come again; but not before
A time set down, or sent for by this whore.
Nor durst he come; (oh! this a hellish prize)
For fear that she should scratch out both his eyes.
Fresh customers are come, she must have time,
To tell her money, and to act her crime.
Muse, stay not here, thou have no hope to mend her:
Go out and see, what money he will send her.
He Would take up money, promise any rate,
But here is the spite, his word is out of date.
He tries a second, he no coync can borrow;
A third denies him, to increase his sorrow.
He cannot choose, but see his credit's lost:
He frets, and swears, Was ever man thus crossed?
But then at last, he thought upon his chest,
Where he had clothes; one sure above the rest,
Being very costly, must to Brokers hall,
And be condemned, to free himself from thrall;
He took the gold, and sent it to his trull;
Judge, had he wit? or were his brains so dull
To think it fit, to plaster up this sore
To bleed a frcsh? it cost him six times more.
The whisk in brings the gold; and now this Jade,
Does bless her fortunes, such a thriving trade
None but my self enjoys! how am I blessed!
I live at ease, I eat and drink the best!
My house well furnished, I have joy my fill,
I go in silk, here is gold, more's coming still;
O sweet! O pleasing! profitable sin!
To raise me thus! now I can raise my kin:
I Have brave attendants, one wears silver lace,
The other scarlet, is not this a grace?
Now here is a calm, the blustering Devils laid,
The evil spirit's conjured; hell is staid,
From belehing fury: is it not very strange?
'Twill vent again, when once the Moon does change:
The Pimps must have their sees, need makes'em crave it,
To buy them boots; she is loath to let them have it,
Although her gold does ring such lusty sounds:
Her trading's raised from eighteen-pence to pounds.
It Is but a time! will she be ever young?
Her pounds will fall to nine-pence er't be long.
Age and the P. will shame her shameless lise;
Or hell will arbitrate, and end the strife.
But where is the lecher all this while, that I
Have left so long? thoughts of Adultery,
Have made him drunk; or is he raging mad,
To think upon the pleasure that he had?
And how he is deprived? or of the time
When he shall go once to renew his crime?
Or does he count the minutes of the clock?
Or enjoy time, and wish himself a Stock?
Or does he take an hour for a day?
Or thinks the day a year? or does he stay
In The Country for the air? or in the City,
Expecting still when she will show him pity
To send for him? and think that hour is blessed?
For that hours sake, he will chide, and curse the rest.
The time's not come, she set; nor has he treasure,
He dares not go 'pon pain of her displeasure;
Unless he would sell his eyes, or pawn his face
Against her nails; it is money buys the place.
His heart is dead, although his grief survive;
His body tombs his mind, while he is alive.
The time is tedious, for it will not run;
Nor change the scene, until its part be done.
The place yields no content, nor will he tarric
In any place, his fancy still does vary.
No company can give to him delight,
Except the queen; O! were she in his sight
To put his mind in tune, his grief were past:
H'has nere been merry since he saw her last.
The faithless Broker, on the other hand,
Does fret him too; he will not take his band,
And let him have his suit; no, he must pay,
Or if he does not fetch it at the day,
'Twill be condemned again; so he is perplexed:
The Broker and the whore, though he be vexed,
Show him no favor; how is he distressed!
He Is no more piti'd than a savage beast.
Had he but money, then the Pimp would find him,
And flatter him, and swear, how she did mind him
At every turn; and open such a story,
That how she counted him her chiefest glory.
But now he fears, he will bring some other to her,
That Is flush with gold: and help him then to woo her.
Alaspoore man! why do not think as well,
The way to her is the right way to hell?
Think how Calisto, in his heat of lust,
Beat out his brains; think how this whore will rust,
And moult away: Paulina was more fair,
Dukes did embrace her; like the gilded air
She did appear; she to the Hangman gave
Herself a Prostitute; that she might have
But one meals meat; sh'was afterward denied
A piece of bread; with guilt and shame she di'd.
Cast up thy gain, see how it does amount;
The more thou sinn'st, the greater's thy account.
How vain! how short! how cursed are thy contents!
Take them at best, they are but merriments,
That bring forth shame, guilt, everlasting horror;
Art thou not mad, that thou do thus grieve for her?
Would thou fain have thy children beg their bread?
To hate thee living, and to curse thee dead?
Now turn betime, while yet thou have a space;
For after death, thou wilt find nor time, nor place.
SECT. 27. Of the life, death, and funeral of a Gentleman, undone by a brood
of vermin.
AMournfull accident I must unfold!
I Will speak but that which prating Fame has told:
A Gentleman well bred (for by relation,
His friends were famous for their reputation)
Brought up to learning, never sparing cost;
But yet it was but charge, and labor lost.
For why? his lewdness hurried on his fate,
He followed whores, so was unfortunate.
What e're he got, 'twas spent, and all his care,
Was to grow careless; he would never spare,
But laid it on; for this, who can but see,
That whores are known the costli'st beasts that be?
Much thus he spent, and often he was supplied;
But of his will he was at last denied.
His father found all was but thrown away,
The more he had, the more he went astray.
He to reclaim him, strived to keep him shorter;
It changed him not, but put his mind to torture.
Advice, instructions, promises to woo him,
The likely danger put he often unto him;
Then threatening him, that for his foul demerit,
He Would cast him off, and would him disinherit.
All would not serve, he is so far from mending,
That he is more vile, though not so much in spending.
But tenderhearted parents needs must love
The child, though bad; their bowels still'did move.
Though he was shameless (sin did never grieve him)
Yet still his father could not but relieve him.
He Would often cry out, This unhappy Boy
Has shamed my glory, and o'rethrowne my joy:
I am in doubt, if I his part should take,
I shall be deemed as guilty for his sake:
If I neglect him, though he be so vilde,
I shall be censured too; he is my child.
My grief is great, yet all my hope's not past,
Though he goes far, yet he may turn at last.
How should I joy, to see him turn and thrive!
I Would think my self, the happiest man alive.
My hope dies now; while I fresh hopes would borrow,
Fear threatens me, to aggravate my sorrow.
This Russian values not (as it appears)
His fathers mourning, nor his mothers tears.
Nor weeping of his sister, nor his brother;
Nor yet the counsel of his friends, nor other:
But such as are like-minded with him; they
He Will hug, although they take him as a prey.
His Comrades are such Roarers, you may well
Think they were newly vomited from hell.
For like a gallant of great Pluto's Court,
With borrowed language, one must make him sport:
Although he be a bird fit for a cage,
Yet, if he can but parrat on the stage,
He Is now a Gentleman, the other can coin
New oaths, and swear, This oath at first was mine.
He swears in fou^o, domineering in it;
Hell would elect him Master of the Mint.
Give him the wall, or else he will draw, or stab,
He Is made an Usher to a painted Drab.
His wealth hangs on his back (let us have no mocks)
His riches lie in his Tobacco-box.
He Is valiant! pity it is he wants a sword,
The Gentleman must buy, or pass his word
To furnish him: and then he will brag and boast,
And hack and hew, as mad Tom did the post.
His clothes are all put into Tailors books,
He Will hardly pay, I guess it by his looks:
The Tavern is the place where he will abide;
His sword does hang so neatly by his side,
He looks upon it; it joys him at the heart;
Then knocks, and cries; Rogue, bring the other quart;
But has no coin: it is enough that he
Vouchsafe to grace them with his company.
Then having paid, and drank a round carouse,
They went to revell in a bawdy house,
(As'twas supposed) but there they fell at strife;
The Gentleman ('it was thought) did keep his wife,
And he consented to it; but who can tell,
Whether or no? but this, all was not well.
The wine did work, which stirred up angry words,
From words to blows; and having drawn their swords,
The quarrel grew so hot (and so it past
Between them all) that it fell out at last,
This gallant there was slain; a little wound
Spoiled his delight, and brought him to the ground.
A fearful thing! but e'rehe lost his breath,
He struggeld much with that grim Tyrant, Death,
Confessed his faults, but when his friends came in,
Shame forced him thus to aggravate his sin.
Alas! my sorrow cannot be expressed!
A hell, a hell is gendred in my breast!
My hateful sins have brought me to this case,
And every one seems with a Devils face:
I lived a beast, O! that I might so dye;
I wish in vain! worse than a beast am I!
I scorned advice, now I am scorned of all,
The world may now rejoice to see my fall.
My soul is sunk; it must go, who knows whither?
I Would live, yet dye, and yet I would do neither!
My heat encreast, though I did often drench it:
But now I find, my blood alone will quench it.
Reproach for ever seizes on my name,
And for my sake, my friends must bear the shame.
How short are vain delights! how soon they are gone!
They show content, but yet they give us none.
Vnseason'd pleasures! what is it that they bring,
But guilt? they pass, and bear a dreadful sting,
Which time will never wear out. Alas! this story
Is but a catalogue of painted glory!
I pleased my self with sensual things; and lies
I took for truth: all good I did despise.
I see my folly, how am I too blame!
These cursed contents have but a rotten name.
Thou great Preserver of the souls of men,
Show mercy on me: and thy praises then
Shall be declared by all that know thy love:
And I shall bless thee with thy Saints above.
Vile world adieu! O heavens, take my breath!
And snatch my soul out of the jaws of death!
Thus spoke, he ended; all his breath was spent:
Death seized on him, and closed him in his tent.
To show the case his body then was in,
The consequent of his precedent sin,
I now forbear; I Will give you leave to think
What whores do bring, with great excess of drink.
His friends lament him; how their tears do fall:
They Will all be spent before the funeral.
His mother chiefly lays to heart this cross;
With brinish tears she thus bewailes her loss.
O heavens! how has this black horrid fate
Made such a blessing so unfortunate!
Had he but di'd an infant; or my womb
That brought him forth, had been his senseless tomb,
Both had been happy; how had I been blessed!
For in my grave I had been left at rest.
But now I am tormented; this my sorrow,
Until death come, will never find a morrow.
I see on hopes the more I set my mind,
The less of comfort I am like to find.
Who had more hopes than I had of a son?
Who has less joy? alas I am undone!
Is this a token given by a friend,
To bring my boy to an untimely end?
More she had spoke; but being discontented,
Grief called for tears, and so she was prevented.
The Cause of this his death, how he was slain,
Whose fact it was, all is evident and plain.
Who'd stand upon it? the Sessions would decide it,
But that I think they have already tried it:
He Is coffined up; now this is all I crave,
Provide some tears, and drop them on his grave!
I would not beg, but that his friends have none;
Yet in a week, they will pay you ten for one.
Grave, now receive him; I Have discharged my trust.
Conceal his name: and cover him with dust.
His Epitaph.
HEre lies a man, I can no whit commend him,
He shamed himself in living like a beast;
While he had cash, his Imps would then defend him:
Now he is charged, to make the worms a feast.
His lust, his life, that was so proud and stout,
How soon 'twas with a rapiers point let out!
SECT. 28. Of an old man, wooing, and marrying of a young girl; how they
disagreed, and how she turned a whore.
THere lived a man, (but where I need not tell)
For credit, worth, esteemed of all so well,
That fame began to prate on it: for his life,
To make it sweet, he wanted but a wife:
Death robbed him of his first; she is in her tomb,
Had he another to supply her room,
How happy he should be! for being old,
He must have one, to keep him from the cold.
Wealth's not sufficient, to cheer his age:
He Is newly entering on the seventh stage,
Of bald swift time; his hair with snow was mixed,
But 'twas at odds, for there were paths betwixt,
The hoary-frost, had seized upon his beard,
His face was pale, and wrinkled: you have heard,
How Northern Boreas, from the pole does glide,
To shake his treasure, over the Western bride.
The plants do tremble, Winter is his guest,
Of all the months, December suits him best,
So is his flesh grown chill, his bones do quiver,
Some young one that is warm, must him deliver;
From his cold palsy: O! how feign he would find
One qualified agreeing to his mind!
He sought a while, but 'twas his hap at last
To spy a Virgin, as the stroets he past;
And as he looked, his heart began to melt,
His colour's changed; the passion that he felt,
Did warm his blood: his shaking fit grow's more,
But altered quite; not cold, as 'twas before.
Has Winters Sun now got such power here?
Or will it be thus warm, but once a year?
Frost has no heat in^^ whence then comes this change:
The air is cold, the alteration's strange;
Nor was there sign of fire, how comes this frost
Dissolved so soon? 'twas by a walking ghost.
Sh'had made him young again, if she had staid:
But now he will never be well, till she be laid.
This old man bends his mind, t'obtaine his ends,
H'has hope to have her, 'cause he knows her friends,
He Will go to try; if he be welcome to her,
He Is mad for ever; but his gold must woe her.
He wants a face, he fears she will abhor him;
But yet he hopes, his wealth will do it for him.
Wealth is a Lady; no one will despise her:
But if she does, he knows, her friends are wiser.
He Is gone to try; I wish him good success:
And all that know the man, can do no less.
He speaks but little; that is best at first;
The sight of here is enough to quench his thirst:
Yet he must kiss her; had you seen them play,
You would have laughed: she cried, old man, away.
He must embrace her: O, this precious pearl!
Fie, fio, said she, an old man kiss a girl?
Her friends approved on it; he has hope to speed,
He thinks in time the girl would be agreed.
Home he returns; but ere he goes again,
He Will change his visage: though his habit's plain
He Will have it altered; though his beard be made
To grow so broad, and squared like a spade,
It must be shaved away; and it must be
Turned up, and sharp'c just like a Roman T:
His cloak bag, breeches too, shall be cut less,
He will be suited, in a comely dress.
To suit the rest, he wears his second Ruff,
'Twas made of Cambrick, or some dainty stuff.
His hat's cut narrower, and it is nearly dressed:
(Good husbandry.) He means to keep his best,
Until the wedding: Lovely he had bin,
To look upon, had he but changed his skin.
He Is gone without his staff; he wants no prop,
The worst thing is, (being cold) his nose does drop.
He courts this lovely less, with great delight,
She finds his drift, and then she loathed his sight.
At last he broke his mind to her, and said;
I have a suit to thee: I am a fraid,
Thou will deny me: this is all I crave,
Thy love, thy love, O! that is the thing I Would have:
Sweet honey, grant me this, and I will prove,
So kind to thee, that I Will deserve thy love.
I Have wealth enough, I Will make it all thine own:
If I can have from thee this favourshown,
I shall be blessed; am I too old for thee?
I Am not so old though as I seem to be.
What Is forty years? my wife death took away;
I grieved so much, that made my hair turn gray.
Thou Wilt find me young, and active, never fear;
Be thou but kind; this Diamond-ring, my dear,
Take as a pledge: come, never blush, but speak
Thy mind in love; or else my heart will break!
Young men are fickle, care less of their wives,
Men that are stayed, do love them as their lives.
Thy friends do like it well, if I may find
Thy liking too, O! how 'twill ease my mind!
Then he was silent; but the maid replied:
Sir, you mistake the mark; or shoot too wide.
Does gray hairs dote? how do you think I can
(Being young and fresh) affect so old a man?
In policy you have these words expressed
To try my wit: I take it but in jest.
Love's out of date with you; it is rather meet,
You buy you coffin, and your winding sheet.
I thank you for your love; your wealth, your gold
Provokes me not: if I may be so bold,
I Will say you were not wise, to set your mind,
'Pon one so young: but love they say is blind.
If needs you will wive, then take a marron grave;
Decrepid age, with wealth might comfort have.
Cease, cease your suit; judge, is it a fitting thing
To match cold Winter, to the wanton Spring?
My blood's now rising, yours is in the fall,
You Would do me wrong, your self no good at all.
What good wilt be to you to do me harm?
You Would have a young one, now, to keep you warm.
I cannot think my friends will give consent
To sell me to a living punishment.
Farewell old Father; age might make you know,
Seeing you are rich, you should not look so low.
He hearing this, it cut him to the heart;
But could not speak a word, to case his smart:
His joints did shake, his eyes bewraid their lust;
Then to her friends went he, now speak he must:
Alas! this peevish wench, does slight my proffer,
My love, my person, wealth, whatere I offer
As tokens of good will, are not respected;
The giver, and the gift, are both rejected.
Persuade her but to love me; then will I
Invest her to my wealth: and when I die,
I Will leave her all; if she refuse to give
Her love to me, long here I cannot live.
Then they well weighing, of his great estate,
The credit that he had, how fortunate
She might be in the Match; how they might save,
A sum of money by it; he did not crave
A portion, but her love he aimed at most:
She in his riches; he in her would boast:
They sum'd up all; for they had many ends,
She being raised, she might prefer her friends,
They use persuasions, arguments to move her,
With promises; and how the man would love her.
But threatening was the burden of the song;
She blushed, she wept, and yet she held her tongue.
But discontented, to her self she said:
In what a case am I! I am afraid
I shall be lost for ever. I had rather
Displease my self, than move, and vex my father.
One I must do; I shall be counted vilde,
If he refuse to own me for his child.
Nor will he give me means; I shall be poor,
And if I beg my bread, from door to door
Who Will pity me? if I this old man take,
His wealth can never restitution make
To my afficted mind! how shall I do
To ease my heart, and please my father too?
His age will shame my youth, how shall I meet
My old acquaintance, as I walk the street,
Without a blush? his wrinkled flesh, his breath,
Will be to me as similies of death.
Oh, who would match young blood with parched dust?
Or marrow with dried bones? alas! I must!
Time bring my doom! that is all the hope I have,
That I shall lay him quickly in his grave!
So she submits, and now her Fathers pleased,
The match is made, the Seigniors mind is eased.
But she is grieved; her grieving is in vain:
For it is too late to ease her of her pain.
My Muse was charged (I Am loath that she should stray)
To bid the guests, against the wedding day.
Nor show th'apparrell that they did provide,
To fit the Bridegroom and his lovely Bride.
The preparation for the marriage feast,
What company, who was the chiefest guest.
What mirth they raised, yet how the Bride was sad,
What plate was there, th'attendance that they had.
Nor of the Music, how they played, and sung,
And bore their parts, or how the bells were rung.
What they agreed, their walking after dinner,
Their merry tales, or who was the beginner:
Nor how they supt; the possets at the last;
How he rejoiced, when once the day was past.
Or how the Bride was loat'h to go to bed,
(She was not jealous of her maidenhead.)
How she desired, that her friends would stay,
How he thought long, before they went away.
With sundry other things, if I rehearse,
They'ld rob me of my time, and stuff my verse.
Black Morpheus covered them, old Time did place'em;
But Hymen being angry, would not grace'em.
Dame Nature's vexed at those that made the match:
Her choler's turned to craft, and she does watch,
To do'em a mischief; she the marriage cursed,
She scorned to come to it, though sh'was bid at first.
The Fates are bent against him, in their rage,
They Will graft his head, then bring him on the stage.
They had been married but a little space,
Ere he grew jealous; and he did disgrace
Her in his carriage; he did mind the earth,
And dig'd for gold; she pleasure loved, and mirth.
He was so harsh to her, and did deny
Her things convenient; he was in her eye
Slighted as much; for he was froward, mad;
And feared he had lost, that love he never had.
His fire is quickly quenched, but there is a flame
Of lust in her: nor can he quench the same.
He Is growing downward, she is rising still,
He Would keep her in, but she will have her will.
She calls for money, but he keeps her short;
And sitting by her self, being all amort,
Thus she complains: oh, how am I distressed!
Undone for ever! and my mind's oppressed!
Had death but married me, I had not seen
These days of griese, or matched with age, and spleen.
Oh! that my tender flesh, my blood, and bones,
Were raked in earth, and coverd over with stones!
I should be freed from this cold piece of lead,
That chils my flesh, lies by me in my bed.
Oh! who would suffer such a thing but I?
My temper's hot, and moist, he is cold and dry.
Why should I spend my youthful days in sorrow?
Or stay death's leisure, waiting for a morrow?
Shall I alive, be penned up in my grave?
Mewd up at home, to please this hide-bound slave?
I Will never do it: Lycurgus made a law
To break such matches; and to keep in awe
Such jealous fools: wise Solon did consent
That woman which at home had no content,
Might have some other friend should freely do
Without controwle, what she consented to.
What pity it is, these Laws are out of date!
That could not choose, but be a happy State!
This help I have, I know he is precise,
(Though he be crafty, I will be as wise.)
I Will be so too: I Will go to Church, nay more,
I Will read, I Will pray, I Will seem to go before
The strictest of them; many things I Will do,
Lift up mine eyes; both lie, and flatter too.
He Will never distrust me, then I shall be known,
I Will get acquaintance, and I Will use mine own.
Nature has made me perfect, I was borne
To please my self, and give this Churl the horn.
I shall have gold enough, love, and delight,
When men of worth take pleasure in my sight.
This hypocrite is troubled in her mind,
Dissembles cleanly; if he will be kind,
And let her go, that she may comfort take,
Sh'has sworn to turn a Convert for his sake.
He Is glad; these times to her he will allow,
He does not doubt, but she is a Convert now.
She Is manned along, she is gone, (he does not fear)
To see and to be seen, but not to hear.
When she comes home, she is so grieved for sin,
She cannot tell the text; but she will begin
To change her life: it is true, you may believe her,
She has not sinned enough, and that does grieve her.
She Is provident, she will do the best she can,
To bring her mind to go without a man.
She Would seem to go to Church, but mist the door,
Because her mind was bent to play the whore.
Her customers kept touch; nor did she stay,
But Sermon-time; a game, and so away.
Her forward zeal did please the old man's mind,
Although it burned amiss; he could not find
Out her deceit: before the bells had rung,
She Would still be gone, and staying somewhat long,
At last he did mistrust, and sent a spy,
Who being cunning, with a watchful eye,
Found out her tract; and took her in the lurch;
For of a bawdy house, she made her Church.
This news being brought, oh, how this man lamented!
His passion stopped his mouth: so discontented!
His griefe's to great, to be expressed with tongue,
Or melted into tears, shame's mixed among.
When she came home (poor whore) (she had no doubt
That he mistrusted, or had found her out.)
She seemed to be much taken with the preacher,
And for the sermons sake, she praised the teacher.
She could rehearse the doctrine, and the use,
But left the reasons out; her foul abuse
Is now discovered: yet she dos deny it;
He Is but a knave that says it; I defy it.
But being proved so plainly to her face,
And where she was; she altered then her case.
Not by submission; but she was more bold:
And told her husband; sir, you are too old
For my young years; you cannot give delight;
I like you ill by day, but worle at night.
Age makes you senseless; just like wood, or stone,
You take up room, but all your vigor's gone.
Besides, you are so fretfull, and so froward,
So hide-bound, jealous, dogged, and untoward,
I cannot love you; I must take my pleasure,
I am too quick, to tarry your dull leisure.
You knew my mind before, yet you would crave
My person; but my love you could not have.
You urged my friends, and they prevailed with me
To give my hand; my heart did never agree.
Was not your act in that, as bad as this?
Pray pardon me then, though I do amiss.
He staid not to reply, nor to relate
How by her means, he was unfortunate;
Nor of her former carriage, nor that act,
Though her dissembling did increase the fact.
But he in fury opened wide the door,
And turned her out; now she is a licenst whore.
She Is known abroad, and she willtrade with many,
And by degrees with sharking rogues, or any,
That are but young; she will freely share a stake:
But with no old man, for her husbands sake.
Within the city she does first begin
To set up for her self; and truck with fin.
She keeps all open, and she freely can
Bid welcome to the master, or the man.
But yet she stays not long, in any place,
She Will change her name, and often change her face.
Yet now and then, the prisons crave a fee,
They'are largely paid by such a one as she.
Some by her, lost their credits; some their health,
Some lost their souls (I fear) and all their wealth.
Men were bewitched by this deluding sinner:
And when she found her hel-black troop came thinner,
She left the city walls, though there 'twas fading,
She thought the skirts would yield her constant trading,
When she had found a guest, that pleased her eye,
She never left him, till sh'had drawn him dry.
But walking by her self, two sparks did dog her,
And one of them was but a petty-fogger:
Of true-bred whore-masters; he had the strain,
He Had shameless speech, his nose was died in grain:
He wanted wit, and money, but his hope,
Because he had relation to the Pope,
He should be fortunate, and welcome to her:
And any friendly office he can do her,
(Put coin aside) she shall command him still,
All that he craves, is only her good will,
That he may wait upon her; to a minut,
He Will do her service; she may trust him in it.
She entertaines this servant of renown,
But with his Mistress he is familiar grown,
And very inward; (yet if you had spied him,
You would have thought the Hospital had fri'd him.)
Pretending business at her chamber late,
And e're she rose: they two must lie and prate.
What else they did at any time, or place,
I Will leave it on the score: time will not race
The shame away: they all the debt must pay,
Or here below, or at the Judgment day.
This letcherous Trull is careless of her name,
Forgets her soul, and makes a jest at shame.
She has her choice of Rascals; among the rest,
One seasoned whore-master does like her best:
He Is very brave, although he be not rich;
His colour's like to carrion in a ditch.
His life does stink as bad; he had a wife,
But whilst she lived, she had a begging life.
He spends his means and time to keep this whore,
And for her sake, he will make his children poor.
His friends forsake him, he is led by sense,
He Is scorned of all men; that is his recompense.
The Cancars make him vex, it is worst of all,
They visit him at every spring and fall.
They search within, and creep up in their kind,
To seize upon his eyes, and make him blind.
This queen does carry't smoothly; with her tongue
She does deceive men, modest looks among
Are mixed with her deceits; and with the best
She strives to dwell, and make her whorish nest:
And goes in modest habit; none can know,
But that she is honest by the outward show.
With whores it is grown in fashion in our times,
With seeming modesty to hide their crimes.
Where ere she comes, her promises are large,
If custom fail she cannot pay the charge.
If she be forced, her lodging to remove,
They must believe: she will leave her empty love
Behind in pawn; this pawn is cheap and ill:
But if she pay not, they must keep it still.
Long she continued in this shameful course,
No alteration, but from bad to worse.
The old man tries all means to change her mind,
All is but labor lost; nor can he find
Content without her; he his time had past
With discontent; Death darted him at last.
And now she is joviall; all her debts she will pay,
(If they can get'em) when he is clothed in clay:
To time I Will leave her, she must take her turn
By death e're long. Now he is laid in his urne.
His Epitaph.
THis Seignior sold his joy, to please his lust;
He did repent on it, when his heat was quenchs;
Time judged it fitter he should match with dust,
When he with brinish sorrow had been drencht.
Dame Nature envies all unequal matches:
Death, for his fault, does keep him under hatches.
SECT. 29. Of a proud, stately Harlot raised by her sin; how she rained diverse;
and of many passages in the prosecution of it.
THe gallant whore, who goes in rich array,
Turns day to night; the night she will turn to day:
Although at first her breeding was but base,
Sprouts as a branch from some Imformers race.
Being somewhat handsome in her blushing prime,
She thinks it best, to make use of her time.
Some braying Ass that can but use his tongue,
He Will complement: they being both but young,
Are soon be witcht with one another faces;
Then for their ends they have their meeting places,
Till at the last this Ass had shed his coat,
His braying alters to a mourning note.
The Sergeants, Bailiffs, do increase his fears,
Poor Ass at last does prick, or shake his ears,
And thinks his heelcs are better than his hands,
He whips away into the Netherlands.
This Queen adornes her like a modest Maid:
Alas! another is by her betrayed!
Now she being cunning, will not serve his lust;
His fire burns, and marry her he must:
A little while they do in pleasure swim;
At last he finds she made an Ox of him,
He then complained: Alas! what did I mean,
To marry thus an over-ridden queen?
His way of trading he does quite neglect,
His Creditors will give him no respect.
His stock's confirmed, his credit all is lost:
Poor simple man! he with this whore is crossed!
She lays it on, and spends (while it remains)
On such that promise to allow her gains.
At last, when he had run so far in debt,
He Would buy some wit, but it is too late: he will fret:
His wit and money parted are away,
Now he must run, he can no longer stay.
The time is come this whore did wish to have,
She Will have more freedom with a perjured knave:
She Will trim her self, and paint her face anew:
She Is brave! her face looks of another hew.
Come now who will, this block is far removed:
You are the man that I have ever loved.
Then she will swear that she will be constant to him;
She works him in, but quickly she will undo him.
He must maintain her at a lofty rate;
She goes in pomp; at last her whorish state
Does bring him low; his house he keeps no longer,
He is conveyed to one that is far stronger.
Then he bewailes his lamentable cross;
And does relate their passages; his loss
('It Is evident, poor man! he must abide it)
Breaks out the more, the more he strives to hide it.
Now when this whore does want a man of worth
To keep her brave, she will send her Pander forth.
(Pray call him Captain) she must bear his charge;
Her streams are low, expenses have been large.
Well, out he goes, being hindered by no weather;
His money's spent, but he will shake his feather:
He finds the Spark, he tells him of a Less,
That does for beauty all the rest surpass.
If he would but go, he shall be welcome to her,
She Is somewhat coy; but he will help to woo her.
These joviall blades must make a merry day,
To talk more on it: the reckoning called to pay,
Pimps purse is empty; but's Tobacco-box
To all is free (they would best beware of pox)
In open glasses they must drink the round,
Then suck the weed of eighteen pence a pound.
Now Whisk in brings this joviall gallant up,
A place appointed where they all must sup:
And for their sauce, they have this painted lade
Brought in; he sees her, scone the match is made:
They vow then to be constant to each other:
She is his sister, he must be her brother:
And while he lives, he never will for sake her:
But for his wife, he will bid, The Devil take her.
Now for his whore; her back and belly cheer,
Do cost him full five hundred pounds a year:
Maintains the Pimp that brought him to this thrall:
No other Bawd; she is Whore and Bawd and all.
Now farewell Freeze, h'has got to grace this harlot,
A Stammell suit; but you must call it Scarlet.
In habit like a Citizen she will be;
Sometimes no Lady is more fine than she.
A house for winter, she must have to stand
About the City, with a brave command:
Her Summer House near to the flowing tide,
Rich furniture, gained by her whorish pride.
Now when this Spark is from his Fondling gone,
If none come to her, then she will send for one;
Bring her but money, she will use her trade,
And truck with any, like a Hackney lade.
When he returns, she will wipe her mouth, and say,
Why did my sweeting stay so long away?
He must embrace her, kiss, and call her honey;
She Will quickly search to see what store of money
He'has brought her home; if little, then she will frown,
She wants a satin, or a tabby-gown.
Up he must take it on the Mercers score,
And glad he is that he can please this whore:
And of the Goldsmiths jewels, diamond-rings:
When this is done, still she wants other things.
His state consumes, his friends give council to him
With grief: and plead, this whore will soon undo him.
All will not serve, but he will still adore her.
His wife now sees a queen preferred before her,
With heavy heart does to her husband cry:
Forsake this whore, or I with grief shall dye!
With tears she prays, as she before him stands,
You have an heir, then morgage not your lands.
Alas! you know, 'twill be a living shame,
When he is an heir only but in name!
These infants, they must beg, or else do worse,
You on your children strive to bring a curse:
Shall it be said, this was his fathers place?
He kept a whore, and sold it with disgrace?
Your of-spring-too shall ever bear this slain,
You raised them up, and threw them down again,
Think on that vow you did in marriage make,
With that injunction; which was, then to take
None but my self; you know I am your wife:
This Harlot now has gained your love; sowed strife
Betwixt us two: what is love gone for ever?
Which once I thought, that none but death could sever?
My self, my love was constant still and true;
My friends I did forsake for love of you;
Oh! be persuaded, pity now my smart;
And toward your children, bear a fathers hear?
If to my suit you will not bend your cares,
Let grief dissolve me to a flood of tears!
When thirsty time has drank up all my store,
Then take me death: for I can weep no more;
And let me now a second favor find,
My children take, which I shall leave behind.
And for my husband, let all honest men
Lay out some tears; he will one day pay'em again.
Until that time, I wish, that babbling Fame,
Be silenc't that he never spread his name.
This one thing more (my passion is so strong:)
And then for ever I will hold my tongue:
Some Poet he will write upon your grave,
He kept a whore who used him as a slave,
To maintain her, he did both sell and borrow;
His children beg, his wife she died with sorrow.
Let time and age with men record his fall:
Be warned by him; his whore and he spent all.
This plaint moves not; nor infants moanes about her
He Is vexed at her, and sometimes he does flour her;
Then he relates this story to his drab,
Who answered thus; what, has she eat a Crab?
What, does she prate? a stinking durty slut!
Is she too full? I vow I Will pinch her gut,
And make her glad to eat a piece of bread;
What will she dye, except she may be head?
I Will keep her bare and make her speak me fair;
You must be tutored too about your heir.
Tush, let him work, and set him to a trade;
Pack out the rest: and let this ugly lade
Vex in her grease; what, does she send about
Her privy spics to find our meeting out?
Do, be a fool! and let her railing pass,
And show thy self a tender-harted ass!
What did thou say when she did call me whore?
Pray Love, be quiet. I Will do so no more?
She rail at me! Oh! would I could but meet her
In place convenient: see how I could greet her.
Pray if you love me, make her rule her tongue;
She Is old, and wrinkled, I am fresh and young.
A snotty Jade! I mar'le, how thou could love her!
She Is vexed that I have got respect above her.
Let any one that has but skill to try,
Judge which is handsom'st, whether she or I:
Let none compare me to this homely Ioane!
She sreets because she is saine to lie alone.
Who'ld lie by such a whither'd piece of flesh,
When he may have well favored, sweet and fresh?
Go to thy dow'd, if thou have but a mind:
And quit forget that I to thee was kind.
Sweet, do thon think that I will be so mad,
That ere her words or tears shall make me sad?
No, do not think I have so little wit:
Let, let her swell in this her frenzy fit.
Shall I be ty'de? I Will tame this peevish fool.
And for het tongue, let winter make it cool;
Let night-hags fright her in her bedlam dreams,
Whilst thou and I delight in pleasant theames!
I Will take my pleasure. Why should I do less?
I Will be no slave to what I do possess.
Come, thou art mine; and thou shall find me fair
My love to thee is more than to my heir;
I Will spend my 'state; a brood of brats I have
Who for my means do wish me in my grave;
Thus far I am resolved, for love of thee:
When I am gone, the world is gone with me.
Thou have my heart, thou know I cannot leave thee:
I am too honest ever to deceive thee.
Hanged, let her chat until her tongue be weary:
Care kills a cat, but we will still be merry:
But yet I Will use a trick to make her quiet,
She shall go barer, and have meaner diet.
And if she dye, a grave I Will quickly make her,
I love her so; make speed, come, heavens take her.
SECT. 30. Of the woman dying with grief; Her Funeral.
Muse dip thy quill in blood, or tears of mine
Shall change thy ink, and turn it into brine:
Make sad thy self; and when that envious night
Does cast a veil to rob thee of the light
(Being clad in darkness) then do thou begin
To mend thy pen, and bring those Vipers in.
Draw now thy plaint for the poor woman's sake!
And for her children; hark! what moan they make?
Call in those neighbors that have grief, to spare,
To join their hearts, and help to bear a share.
A wife, a mother, is in such distress;
Dry grief nor tears can never it express!
Let strangers come, and pity her, and say
Alas, alas! poor soul she is cast away!
Pray stay a while, (for I invite you all)
'T'attendher corpse unto the Funeral:
You should have wine and comfits; but the cleft
Is grown so barren, that there is nothing left.
Griefburn'd within her, to a raging flame;
No tears were left to qualify the same;
Being spent, she fell; and then she easd her head;
The heaviest living; but the lightest dead.
The Nightingal did to the black bird sing,
And Robin Red-brest spoke no other thing:
The Thrush, the Starling of her death did prate,
The Lark had come too; but it was too late.
All beasts did mourn (but some with grief were pressed)
Save one that joy'd which was a savage beast.
Grave, use her kindly; grief has made thee gain her!
Worms, grumble not, but gently entertain her!
It Is not her fault, you have no better cheer.
Call but a few; 'twill one day cost you dear:
When death comes to arrest you, for her sake:
You for the spoil must restitution make.
Her Epitaph.
VIew well this heap of dust, drop down a tear
To moisten it, let every tender heart
Mourn over this honest woman (call in sear)
I Will praise those that begin, aid bear a part.
Her husband with his whore desired her death,
He moved with pity took away her breath.
SECT. 31. The shameful triumph of the Whore, the Prodigal, and the Pimp.
WE having brought her pined corpse to rest
Hoping her soul's alive among the ble'st.
We'll turn again, and seek the wanton's three,
The Sot, the Whore, the Pimp which merry be,
And drinking wine, the Whore begins to prate:
Whore. Farewell that hag, which did my person hate;
I Will mourn in sack: now she will rail no more,
Nor send her elfes to harken at the door.
She will not whine, nor can she hear us talk,
Nor spy us here, unless her ghost does walk:
Come, drink to me, I Will pledge it over her grave
My honest chuck? a better friend none have!
She spit her venom, owing me a spite;
Thou wast so constant, would not break delight.
Now thou art mine; come, take a thousand kisses!
Black Ioane's not here to keep us from our blisses!

Prod. My sweetest love! thy speech is framed so witty
With eloquence! much like a pleasant ditty:
Thy cherry lips do open with such grace,
Which when I hear, and look upon thy face;
I see a Lilly does the Rose beguile;
Sometimes the rose takes captive for a while
The lilly too: thy nose smells Flora's savor,
Thy eyes like Diamonds, graced with sweet behavior.
I cannot hold! my arms shall now embrace thee,
My love, my person, and my means shall grace thee:
The Bedlam soole is now returned to dust;
She Will come no more to haunt us here, I trust.
Grave, keep her close; for if thou lett'st her see
The light again, I Will make a grave for thee.
Worms, tarry not; creep forward, and grow mad
To eat her flesh; that little that she had.
Earth, show me favor, let no moving tones
Make thee defer, but soon dissolve her bones.
She being dead, and in her grave thus hurled,
I would not see her now for all the world.
Come, come my Io, let pleasure still delight us,
The hag is gone, and will no more affright us.

Pimp. O, bravely spoken! Nature met with art,
Acts both a Tragic, and a Comic part:
So Poetlike! Rhetorical, and sweet,
In lofty terms, graced with a carriage mere!
A rarity grown too much out of fashion!
Sharp, sweet, unseen, and yet to vent a passion!
You speak of love so rarely, and so true,
That Ovid might have come to school to you.
But being cro'st your looks and words are stern,
Democritus is childish and might learn.
Not words alone: but this I ever find
You are inric'h still with a noble mind.
And all your actions savor of the same,
Which with consent, bring honor to your name.
I taste your bounty; and have cause to praise you!
I Will do my best to honor and to raise you.
O, happy journey! happy'st of the rest!
When first I brought you where we now are ble'st!
For what I'promis'd then, you found no less:
A beauty rare! more than I did express!
That witch is gone, which did disturb our mirth,
A prey of envy swallowed by the earth:
The worms are feasting underneath the ground.
Now farewell Hag, thou have thy mortal wound

SECT. 32. How the prodig all was servile to his whore; with the condition of
the Pimp.
Muse, make a stop, and view those ulcer'd blaines
That stink and run along their fester'd veins.
The whore's a fountain, by whose poisoned spring
This Bat, this Owl, they both do sit and sing:
The Owl so soon as he has filled his crop,
Then of this fountain he must taste a drop;
Though it is infected, yet he will often drink,
It glides so smoothly by the swelling brink!
Unwholesome drops! and change does make it thicker:
Yet dearer than ten quarts of whelsome liquor.
The skip-jack bat, must wait to take his prey,
And taste those drops; the owl being flown away.
Both birds of night: the owl he'lhave no taster:
The bat's the servant, and the owle's the master,
They all rejoice: now his deceitful whore,
Does promise love: she aims at one thing more.
She like a frost has nipt a fruitful head;
She loathed her living, and she hates her dead,
And so her mate, to please her does as much.
Oh! who should think the world had any such!
Unnatural brute, to slight a loving wife
To please a whore! whose love will turn to strife!
And flattring Pimp that has no other living,
But by this whore, unless it be by theeving,
To fawn on him (the pains of hell to lend him)
And breath his praise, when shame does still attend him.
No greater curse, to have a villain made
To be familiar; but it is his trade.
He Is grown so brave, and if you heard him prate,
You Would think he were a man of great estate:
He Will take a house, but if you look for rent,
You are deceived: 'twas never his intent
To pay for sleeping; you may have his bill,
Take that or nothing, pay the rent who will.
For any thing, if he can get but day,
The price parts not; he never means to pay.
Ask him for money, he will quite forsake you,
He Will pay with scoffs, and bid the devil take you.
Affront a Captain? go, you cheating knaves,
Mechanic rascals are poor hungry slaves!
Base pesants vcx a gentleman of fashion?
Ask me for coin, to put me into passion?
I Will make you stay. But if you haunt his ghost,
He Will change his lodging: man and money's lost.
This whore being noted, then to hide her shame,
Will often remove, and often change her name.
Amongst her names, there is one would make her blessed
In lier esteem; a lady is the best.
So takes her name, alas! there is a man
Keeps of the watch, she will hang him if she can,
Though It be her husband, what she can she'ldoe,
Lest she her self be hanged for having two.
She Is coacht along; she shortly must (I doubt)
Ride in that box which carries dogs about,
To keep her close; lest some undone quite by her,
Should brush her coat with kanes; if they do spy her,
It Is good enough, but yet it is very bald,
That such a punck should like the dogs be haled.
Her ladyship (for so she would feign be stilde)
Is grown so proud, so impudent, and vilde
That she will curse this coxcombe, like a slave,
He durst not cross her, she her will must have.
If he by chance does cross her, then she will thunder;
He Is fearful, silent, thus she keeps him under.
Then he must beg and flatter to be sriends:
This paultry whore will then propound her ends.
A piece of gold, a jewel, or a ring,
A scarf, a gorget, or some pretty thing
That she will have, so he must buy her kisses,
Yet he is glad, and counts them for his blisses.
Now he is persuaded, she loves none beside him;
Nor from her sight, she cannot well abide him.
He Will make her honest; was it heard besore
That any man could make an honest whore?
Or can a whore be honest? then I Will say,
That black is white, and night is turned to day,
The stones will fly, and fire will downward bend,
And hell dissolve, all things amiss will mend.
Nor do I credit that a whore loves men,
But sor her ends: and so she is cursed again.
His swearing is rewarded with a curse,
Sweet poisoned pills do make his plague the worse,
He dotes, and dos an Idol of her make,
But 'twas more safe, if he embraced a snake.
If one to jeare him, asks with him to sup,
To see his whore, he will so take him up;
And swear she is as honest as his mother,
Or any one that will make up the other.
If he a journey from her do intend,
And leave for her but twenty pounds to spend,
This queen then grumbles; and her throat she will raise,
Pish, what is that? 'twill serve but for four days!
She must have more; she is a costly sister,
He had been happy, if he had but mist her.
Well, he is gone; think how she whets her brains,
To serve her lust, and to increase her gains:
To show the trade she drives with other men,
Would sad my Muse, and quite tire out my pen.
She chides the day, to turn it into night;
Judge bad enough; and then you will judge aright.
SECT. 33. How a Whore drew her sister to lewdness; arguments on both sides.
Custom comes thick; she cannot serve their turns,
Her wil's to do it; with lust her body burns.
Having a sister that is a handsome maid,
By her temptations she is at last betrayed;
Seeing her pomp, her jewels, and attire,
Brave company, the money for her hire:
This silly girl, her honesty, good name,
Does put to sale; and glories in the same.
Come, take my counsel, do not be a fool,
To make a purle; for what thou learn'dst at school,
Will not maintain thee: and thy father's dead,
Left thee no means; sell, sell thy maidenhead,
And be not peevish: thou may have a prize
Will raise thy fortunes, if thou will be wise.
The work is easy, it is delight and pleasure;
Fool, use thine own, and thou shall purchase treasure;
Did thou but know what pleasure there is seated,
Soon would thou yield, thou need'st not be entreated:
The proverb's true, Frost genders not with fire.
To things unknown there is no great desire.
Do, be a drudge; if thou be built for toil,
Go settle to it: and leave the golden spoil,
Which thou might take, if thou would cast a trench:
Thou wast not made to be a kitchin-wench.
For diverse Knights, and Gallants would attend thee,
What e're thou would have, rhey'l either give or lend thee;
Which is all one: what e're it be that men
Do lend me once, I never pay't again.
Alas, what had I? my poverty was known,
As much as thine; now happy winds have blown
Me golden dust: if I had been an Ass,
To Have been so coy to let that season pass,
What had I done? no, no, I had more wit;
Now here thou see, how Lady-like I sit:
My table's furnished richly still with fare,
All which comes free, I never do take care:
My charge of living, plainly does appear,
Amounts unto a thousand pounds a year.
Be ruled by me, I speak because I love thee,
Of all thy kindred none shall be above thee.
It Is for thy sake, that I relate this story,
Believe thine eyes, and think upon my glory.
All honor me, a Knight would feign me wed,
But he'res the spite, that Cuckold is not dead.
What, all amort does not my counsel please thee?
Speak, pettish fool, thy mind; and that will ease thee.
Sister, though I am poor, I hold it no disgrace,
My honestie's my portion; and my face
Is not ashamed; I dare to show it where
You cannot yours, unless it be in fear.
With my condition, I am well contented;
Though you are raised, you are to be lamented!
My labor likes me; but the world can tell,
You go the way which ends at last in hell.
What is your state, for all your costly diet?
It Is true content, to have a conscience quiet.
What should I do, if I commit such evil,
But give my soul and body to the Devil?
What you enjoy, is all but painted glory,
The repetition makes a shameful story.
Should I consent, then all that did me meet,
Would blaze my shame along the open street:
What e're I got, what would avail my store,
When all shall point, and say, There goes a whore?
And when I dye, my sins would then outlive me;
Such gain is loss, and what the world can give me,
Can no whit cool the scalding heat of sin,
Nor bribe the conscience; but it will bring in
A sad relation to increase my smart;
Then pleasing sin will prove a mortal dart.
Pray urge me not, the stones your words do hear,
They melt with passion, and they quake with fear.
The rustling leaves do grumble at your talk,
The trees do threaten vengeance as we walk.
See how the grass is now bedewed with tears,
O're-spred with palenesse, over-charg'd with fears;
The fish did spy us, as they were at play,
And were ashamed, and so they slunk away.
Bright Phoebus too is hid behind a clowd,
To blast your counsel; being not allowed,
The world affords no place for sin to dwell;
T'go out is worse, for'tis to go to hell.
The truth ere long this sinful world shall find,
And have reward, all suited in their kind.
What, grown precise? what conscionable stuff
You trade in now! I know you have enough;
Pray sell me some; and I will pay you well,
But pass your word, it shall me keep from hell,
Conscience, I think, it is you prate about:
He Is hanged long since, to put you out of doubt.
You show your wit; is this for my reward,
To get you freed? and giving you regard?
'Twas merely love to you that did incite me
To do you good; but ill you do requite me.
You saucieslut! is this the thanks I have
To trick you up, and make you fine and brave,
To censure me? and slight me for my pains?
You Will damn me for it, and curse me, and my gains.
Pray get you gone, and if you cannot brook it,
A better place, let conscience go and seek it.
I Will hear no lectures, nor be taught by you,
I know enough: your counsellis so new,
It Is not digested; never see me more,
Nor call me sister, be thou nere so poor,
I Will never own thee; nor the least relief
Will give to thee to qualify thy grief.
Your Maidenhead, and honesty together,
Will feed you then; and cloth you from the weather,
Conscience is but a Tyrant, at the best,
And in distress will never let thee rest.
The Law of Nature bids thus take delight,
And makes things meet to please our appetite.
It kindles love, and breeds desire to it:
Who durst say, It Is not lawful then to do it?
Some Bedlam Stoic did at first devise
(That wanted nature) treason stuffed with lies,
To mew us up. The earth had too much treasure,
When all was free; and all might take their pleasure?
Have birds free leave to change once every year?
And beasts in common 'gender free from fear?
Shall we that once were made to keep them under,
Enjoy less sweet, in being kept asunder?
A learned Poetsutes a Bedlams dream,
And idle nurses, to the conscience theme.
' The less the better then, whence this will fall,
It Is to be perfect, to have none at all.
It Is not for youth, but for declining age,
To act a winter Satyr on the stage.
Frost is till then prodigious, we may do
What lustful youth and pleasure prompts us to.
What say to this, thou froward silly girl?
Which will thou choose, the pibble, or the pearl?
To live in credit? or receive disgrace?
In some poor cottage, still to hide thy face?
Do love the warmth? or do affect the cold?
Gain something young, or beg when thou art old.
Come, learn some wit; my care of thee is such,
That joins with love, which makes me speak so much,
Fear thou not hell, or ever to be cast
From heaven: why? we may repent at last.
This silly virgin now is in distress!
